Maturidi's Method in Defending the Correct Belief on Qada and Qadr
Describes Maturidi's method in defending correct belief about qada and qadr against Jabriyya and Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central methodological principle Maturidi used to defend the correct Sunni position on divine decree and predestination (qada and qadr) against both the Jabriyya and Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Maturidi steers a middle path between fatalism and overemphasis on human autonomy.

Before diving into specific arguments, Imam al-Maturidi sets out his overall approach to the most contentious issue in Islamic theology: qada and qadr. This passage from Kitab al-Tawhid reveals his method — grounding theology in text, intellect, and the science of social and natural realities. It then shows how that method leads him to reject both the Jabriyya's denial of free will and the Mu'tazila's theory that God is obliged to do the most beneficial for humans.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of kalam writings
The passage begins by describing the general method of kalam works: they start with revelation, then the intellect's role, then integrate both to build correct creed. This establishes the framework for Maturidi's own approach.
Notice that Maturidi sees theology as a science that engages with both text and reality.
2
Identify Maturidi's unique contribution
The passage claims Maturidi was pioneering in systematically undertaking this task in Kitab al-Tawhid and his Quran commentary. Note that his approach is considered the first of its kind.
This underscores the originality of his method.
3
See how Maturidi addresses two extreme groups
The passage next explains that Maturidi defended against the Jabriyya (who deny human will) and criticized the Mu'tazila (who diminish divine power by obligating God). This shows his balanced position.
Each group is an opposite error: one denies human responsibility, the other limits divine sovereignty.
4
Recognize the significance of the section's size
The passage ends by noting that the section on qada and qadr takes up over a quarter of the book. This shows the importance Maturidi attached to this topic.
This helps the reader appreciate the depth of his treatment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن المؤلفات التي كُتبت في ميادين علم الكلام قد اهتمت أولاً بالنص ثم العقل المكلف بفهم هذا النص ، يليه الامتزاج بينهما حتى يصل إلى المبادئ التي تكوّن أسس الفكر الإسلامي والبيان بالعقيدة الصحيحة المبنية على الحقائق الطبيعية والاجتماعية، كما أخذت على عاتقها الدفاع عن موقف الإسلام أمام الأفكار والمعتقدات الخاطئة التي ظهرت بالداخل والخارج. وقد رأينا أن الماتريدي أخذ على عاتقه بهذه المهمة في كتاب التوحيد بشكل منهجي وفي تأويلات القرآن بمناسبات عديدة؛ ويعني ذلك أن المهمة هذه التي قام بها الماتريدي في هذا المجال يعتبر الأول من نوعه حسب معلوماتنا اليوم. ففي هذا المجال نرى الماتريدي قد حاول الدفاع عن عقيدته أمام الجبرية التي تنكر الإرادة والمعاملة الحرة للإنسان رغم أنه مكلف، وينتقد المعتزلة بانتقادات شديدة اللهجة حول انتقاص القدرة والإرادة الإلهية حاملة بنظرية الأصلح» أن يجعل من الله مكلَّفا، فحاول بالتالي بناء على المبادئ المذكورة أن يبين العقيدة الصحيحة حول القضاء والقدر ومن الجدير بالذكر أن القسم الذي انفرد بهذا الموضوع في كتاب التوحيد يزيد على ربع الكتاب حجمًا.
Indeed, the writings composed in the fields of kalam (theology) focused first on the text, then on the intellect obligated to understand this text, followed by the integration of both until reaching the principles that form the foundations of Islamic thought and the elucidation of the correct creed based on natural and social realities. They also undertook the defense of Islam's position against false ideas and beliefs that appeared internally and externally. And we have seen that al-Maturidi undertook this task in Kitab al-Tawhid methodically and in Ta'wilat al-Qur'an on many occasions; this means that this task that al-Maturidi carried out in this field is considered the first of its kind according to our information today. In this field, we see al-Maturidi attempted to defend his creed against the Jabriyya, who deny human will and free action despite being obligated, and he severely criticizes the Mu'tazila, with strong criticism regarding their diminishing of divine power and will, carrying their theory of al-aslah (the most beneficial) that makes God obligated. Thus he attempted, based on the mentioned principles, to clarify the correct creed regarding qada and qadr. And it is noteworthy that the section devoted to this topic in Kitab al-Tawhid exceeds a quarter of the book's size.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage introduces Maturidi's handling of the issue of qada (divine decree) and qadr (divine predestination) within the broader context of Islamic theology. It first makes a general observation about kalam literature: it prioritizes the revealed text, then the intellect's responsibility to understand it, and then the integration of both to derive principles of Islamic thought and the correct creed based on natural and social realities. This literature also defends Islam against false ideas from within and outside. The passage then notes that Maturidi undertook this task methodically in Kitab al-Tawhid and in Ta'wilat al-Qur'an, and his work in this field is considered pioneering. Specifically, Maturidi defended the correct belief against two opposing groups: the Jabriyya, who denied human free will and moral responsibility, and the Mu'tazila, who, through their theory of al-aslah (that God must do what is most beneficial), diminished divine power and will by making God obliged. Maturidi aimed to clarify the correct creed on qada and qadr. The passage concludes by noting that this section in his book is very extensive, covering more than a quarter of the book.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that qada and qadr only concerns divine foreknowledge and that human free will is irrelevant.
Correction: Maturidi's approach shows that qada and qadr must be understood in balance with human ethical responsibility, as he rejects both the Jabriyya's denial of free will and the Mu'tazila's overemphasis on human capability at the expense of divine power.

Checkpoint
Which two groups does Maturidi oppose in his discussion of qada and qadr?
A) Jabriyya and Mu'tazila
B) Ash'arites and Jabriyya
C) Mu'tazila and Shi'a
D) Sufis and philosophers

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's approach to qada and qadr is a balanced middle path, defending human moral responsibility without diminishing divine power and will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or setback, do you instinctively lean towards fatalism (thinking 'it was written, so I cannot change it') or do you actively seek to act while trusting in God's wisdom? How does Maturidi's balanced view challenge your default response? What concrete step can you take this week to embody both trust in God's decree and personal responsibility?
?
The Mu'tazila tried to defend God's justice but ended up limiting His power. In your own attempts to make sense of God's actions—whether in your life or in the world—where might you be unintentionally confining God to your own standards of fairness? What does this reveal about your heart's posture towards God's sovereignty?
?
Maturidi devoted over a quarter of his book to clarifying qada and qadr, showing its immense importance. How much time and thought do you actually give to understanding God's decree? If you are honest, is this topic a priority in your faith life? If not, what is holding you back from diving deeper into this foundational creed?

Maturidi's Definitions of Hikma, 'Adl, and Khayr
Defines hikma, 'adl, khayr and refutes dualist/Mu'tazili interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's definitions of wisdom, justice, and goodness, and how they refute dualist and Mu'tazili misunderstandings.

Core Idea
Maturidi grounds theological terms in objective meanings, not subjective benefits.

Maturidi begins his discussion of divine decree by clarifying key terms. He defines hikma, 'adl, and khayr with precise meanings that all Muslims affirm. Then he contrasts these with the flawed definitions of dualists and Mu'tazila, who reduce wisdom to benefit.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very concise style here.
2
Identify the three definitions
Find the definitions of hikma, 'adl, and khayr. Note the opposite of each term (safah, jawr, sharr).
3
Understand the universal agreement
Maturidi states that all Muslims and religious adherents agree that God must be described by these terms and negated of their opposites. This shows the definitions are not controversial.
4
Contrast with dualist and Mu'tazili views
Note how dualists define hikma as 'beneficial to the possessor' and Mu'tazila as 'beneficial to others'. See how they both attempt to assign a cause to divine actions.
This is the core refutation: they reduce wisdom to mere benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن موضوع القدر يعتبر من الناحية موضوع البحث حول عدم تضييق دائرة الاهتمام بالصفات الإلهية وتجهيز الأرضية المناسبة للتكليف الحقوقي والأخلاقي للمكلف، ومن ناحية أخرى يجب الاهتمام به لإيجاد حل لمشكلة الشر . فقد دخل الماتريدي هذا المبحث بالبحث عن المشكلة الأخيرة. ويعنى ذلك أنه يحاول بهذه الطريقة قبل كل شيء أن يوضح بالبيان الشافي لبعض المصطلحات مثل الحكمة التي تعني الإصابة في القول والفعل» وضده السفه، والعدل الذي يعني وضع الشيء في موضعه وضده الجور، والخير الذي يعني التي تأتي بنتيجة حسنة وضده الشر. ومن المعلوم أن المسلمين جميعا، وحتى جميع المعتنقين للأديان، يعتقدون بلزوم وصف الله تعالى بمعاني تلك المصطلحات وتنزيهه بأضدادها. غير أن هؤلاء الذين يتبنون عقيدة الثنائية (الثنوية) يعرفون الحكمة بأنها «الشيء النافع لصاحبه»، وكذلك هؤلاء المنتسبون للاعتزال يعرفونها بأنها «الشيء الذي ينفع الآخر»؛ فهما فرقتان استهدفتا وضع علة للأفعال الإلهية، وفي الوقت نفسه حاولتا من عندهم إيجاد نتيجة لائقة
Indeed, the topic of qadr (decree) is considered from one aspect the subject of research regarding not narrowing the circle of concern with divine attributes and preparing the appropriate ground for legal and moral obligation (taklif) for the morally responsible person, and from another aspect it must be attended to in order to find a solution to the problem of evil. So al-Maturidi entered this investigation by researching the latter problem. This means that he thus tries, before everything, to clarify with a satisfactory explanation some terms, such as hikma (wisdom), which means 'hitting the mark in speech and action' and its opposite is safah (foolishness); and 'adl (justice), which means 'placing a thing in its proper place' and its opposite is jawr (injustice); and khayr (goodness), which means 'that which brings a good result' and its opposite is sharr (evil). And it is known that all Muslims, and even all adherents of religions, believe in the necessity of describing God Almighty with the meanings of these terms and declaring Him free from their opposites. However, those who adopt the dualist creed (al-thunawiyya) define hikma as 'the thing beneficial to its possessor', and likewise those who ascribe to i'tizal define it as 'the thing that benefits others'; thus two sects aimed to posit a cause for divine actions, and at the same time they attempted from themselves to find a suitable result
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage examines Maturidi's discussion of qadr (divine decree) in relation to the problem of evil and divine attributes. It begins by stating that the topic involves a dual concern: first, not narrowing the scope of attention to divine attributes, and second, providing the proper foundation for human moral obligation. A further concern is solving the problem of evil. Maturidi entered this investigation by focusing on the latter. To do so, he first clarifies key terms: hikma (wisdom) is defined as 'hitting the mark in speech and action,' with its opposite being safah (foolishness); 'adl (justice) means 'placing a thing in its proper place,' opposite jawr (injustice); khayr (goodness) means 'that which yields a good result,' opposite sharr (evil). All Muslims and religious adherents affirm that God must be described by these terms and negated of their opposites. However, two sects deviate: the Dualists (thunawiyya) define hikma as 'what benefits its possessor,' and the Mu'tazila define it as 'what benefits others.' Both sects attempt to assign a cause to divine actions and to produce a 'suitable result' from their own reasoning. The passage ends mid-sentence but highlights Maturidi's approach of defining terms before entering theological debate.

Key Terms
الحكمة
العدل
الخير
الشر
السفه
الجور
الثنوية
الاعتزال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That wisdom means whatever benefits God or others, as held by dualists and Mu'tazila.
Correction: Maturidi defines wisdom as objective correctness in speech and action, not subjective benefit.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's definition of 'adl (justice) according to this passage?
A) Giving everyone their due
B) Placing a thing in its proper place
C) Acting according to a set of rules
D) Being beneficial to others

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that divine wisdom, justice, and goodness are objective realities, not mere benefit to possessor or others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or loss, do you secretly doubt God's wisdom because you see no benefit for yourself? How can you actively affirm that God's actions are wise even when they bring you no apparent good?
?
The dualists made God's wisdom dependent on benefit to Himself; the Mu'tazila made it dependent on benefit to others. Do you realize that you sometimes judge God's decrees by how they benefit you or those you love? Will you commit to believing that God's actions are inherently just and wise, regardless of your perceived benefit?
?
This lesson shows that even religious people can distort divine attributes by imposing human standards of what is 'good' or 'fair'. In what specific area of your life are you most tempted to measure God's justice by your own sense of fairness? What would it cost you to surrender that measurement and trust His objective wisdom?

Three Schools on Human Actions: Creation and Acquisition
Enumerates the three positions on human actions and presents Maturidi's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three Islamic theological positions on human actions and Maturidi's middle path that distinguishes between creation and acquisition.

Core Idea
Maturidi's view splits action attribution: God creates, humans acquire.

The question of who creates human actions has divided Islamic theology into three camps. This lesson presents the positions of Jabriyyah, Mu'tazila, and Maturidi, showing how Maturidi's view avoids extremes by distinguishing between God's creation and human acquisition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three schools
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview. Notice that it begins by stating the three schools and then explains each. After reading, review the literal translation.
The passage uses technical terms like khalq (creation) and kasb (acquisition).
2
Understand the Jabriyyah position
The first school, Jabriyyah, asserts that all actions are truly attributed to God. Human attribution is merely metaphorical. This is a deterministic view.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The second school, Mu'tazila, holds that actions are truly attributed to humans. Attribution to God is metaphorical. This emphasizes human free will.
4
Understand Maturidi's position
The third school, represented by Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, affirms that actions are truly attributed to both God and humans: to God as creation (khalq) and to humans as act (fi'l) and acquisition (kasb).
This middle way preserves divine omnipotence and human responsibility.
5
Reflect on the key distinction
Maturidi's distinction between creation and acquisition is crucial. Creation refers to God's bringing the action into existence, while acquisition refers to the human's role in choosing and earning the action.
This avoids the extremes of compulsion (Jabriyyah) and autonomous creation (Mu'tazila).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلف منتحلوا الإسلام في أفعال الخلق الأفعال (الإرادية في ثلاثة مذاهب. فالمذهب الأول هو للجبرية التي تقول بأن الأفعال في حقيقتها تضاف إلى الله، وإلى العباد مجازا. وأما الذي ذهبت إليه المعتزلة هو الرأي القائل بأن أفعال الخلق كلها منسوبة في حقيقتها إلى العباد ونسبتها إلى الله مجاز. وأما الرأي الثالث فهو الرأي القائل بأن الأفعال في معانيها الحقيقية تضاف إلى الله وإلى العباد؛ وهو ما ذهب إليه وتبناه أبو منصور الماتريدي. فالفعل عنده منسوب إلى الله خلقا، وإلى العبد فعلاً وكسبا.
The claimants to Islam differed concerning the actions of creation, the volitional actions, into three schools. The first school is that of the Jabriyyah, which says that actions in their reality are attributed to God, and to the servants metaphorically. As for that which the Mu'tazila held, it is the opinion that all actions of creation are in their reality attributed to the servants, and their attribution to God is metaphorical. As for the third opinion, it is the opinion that actions in their true meanings are attributed to both God and the servants; and this is what Abu Mansur al-Maturidi held and adopted. So according to him, the action is attributed to God as creation (khalq), and to the servant as act (fi'l) and acquisition (kasb).
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage classifies the three Islamic theological positions on human actions. The first is that of the Jabriyyah (fatalists), who hold that all actions are truly attributed to God, and attribution to humans is merely metaphorical. The second is that of the Mu'tazila, who hold that actions are truly attributed to humans, and attribution to God is metaphorical. The third is the position of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, which asserts that actions are truly attributed to both God and humans: to God as creation (khalq), and to the human as act (fi'l) and acquisition (kasb). This middle position avoids the extremes of the other two schools.

Key Terms
الجبرية
المعتزلة
خلق
كسب
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Maturidi's view implies partnership in action between God and humans.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes between creation (khalq) and acquisition (kasb), so God alone creates the action while the human acquires it, avoiding partnership.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, to whom is an action attributed as 'creation' (khalq)?
A) To the servant
B) To God
C) Equally to both
D) To neither

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's position harmonizes divine omnipotence and human responsibility by attributing creation to God and acquisition to humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you tend to blame God (like the Jabriyyah) or take full credit when you do good (like the Mu'tazila)? How would Maturidi's balanced view change the way you respond to your own actions today?
?
If every action of yours is created by God but acquired by you, can you still feel genuine gratitude for good deeds and sincere repentance for bad ones? Commit to a specific situation today where you will consciously reflect on this truth.
?
Is there an action in your life that you have been attributing entirely to yourself or entirely to God? Name it. Then, by Maturidi's framework, how should you now relate to that action—as created by God and acquired by you?

The Three Schools on Human Acts
Maturidi enumerates three schools on voluntary human acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three classical positions on the attribution of human voluntary acts: Jabriyya, Mu'tazila, and Maturidi's via media of creation and acquisition.

Core Idea
Maturidi's kasb theory attributes acts to Allah as creation and to humans as acquisition.

This lesson begins with Maturidi's classification of the three competing schools on human acts. He lays out the extreme determinism of the Jabriyya, the radical free will of the Mu'tazila, and then presents his own balanced position. Understanding these three views is essential to grasp the wider debate on divine predestination and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to identify the three schools. Notice the key terms 'khalq', 'fi'l', and 'kasb'.
2
Compare the schools
Ask yourself: How does each school attribute the act to Allah and the servant? Write a one-line summary for each.
Maturidi's is the only one that attributes both in real senses.
3
Reflect on the middle path
Consider why the middle position might be appealing. It avoids the pitfalls of denying human responsibility (Jabriyya) and the pitfalls of ascribing creation to humans (Mu'tazila).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلف منتحلوا الإسلام في أفعال الخلق الأفعال الإرادية في ثلاثة مذاهب. فالمذهب الأول هو للجبرية التي تقول بأن الأفعال في حقيقتها تضاف إلى الله، وإلى العباد مجازا. وأما الذي ذهبت إليه المعتزلة هو الرأي القائل بأن أفعال الخلق كلها منسوبة في حقيقتها إلى العباد ونسبتها إلى الله مجاز. وأما الرأي الثالث فهو الرأي القائل بأن الأفعال في معانيها الحقيقية تضاف إلى الله وإلى العباد؛ وهو ما ذهب إليه وتبناه أبو منصور الماتريدي. فالفعل عنده منسوب إلى الله خلقا، وإلى العبد فعلا وكسبا. وقد أنكر الماتريدي الاعتراض القائل بأنه حسب الرأي الثالث ينبغي الاعتراف بفكرة كون تحقق الفعل مشتركا بين الله وبين العبد، لأن تصوير خروج الفعل من العدم إلى الوجود وتصميمه في الذهن بجميع تفاصيله، أو أخذ ذلك، فإن الفعل من قدر المحيط والمكان والحد الذي لو أراد أن يعود إليه لا يمكن للإنسان تلاقيه؛ فالشيء الذي في يده عبارة عن التحرك أو السكون نحو المنهي أو المأمور به. فيفهم مما تقدم أن الماتريدي يرى بأن فعلا ما قد فعله الإنسان فله كثير من خاصياته الطبيعية والفيزيولوجية والنفسية، وأن فاعله مهما كان عنده علم بالبعض منها وأنه عامل في تحققها، فهو رغم ذلك يقبل بأن علم الإنسان وقدرته لهما نفوذ محدد أثناء تحقق هذا الفعل بالواقع. ومع هذا الفعل المحدود يأتي بصاحبه إلى مكانة الفاعل بمعناه الحقيقي. وقد قدم الماتريدي هناك مثالا للفعل الواحد الذي يكون في الحقيقة لاثنين، فهو يوجب الشركة، نحو مد اثنين شيئا ينقطع، وإزالة اثنين شيئا عن مكان؛ فيرى في الوهلة الأولى أن فعلهما واحد، والحقيقة هما يصيران بهذا الفعل شريكين لا في الفعل نفسه بل في أنه مفعولهما في الحقيقة، أي في الانقطاع والإزالة. ويعني ذلك أن فعل كل واحد في معناه الحقيقي منسوب إلى فاعله؛ فلا يجوز أن يملك أحد تقوية آخر على فعله، ولا يقدر أن يخلق فعل الآخر. وكذلك لا أحد يقدر أن يفعل فعلا في غير حيزه وغير حال في نفسه. ومن الجدير بالإشارة إلى أن كل واحد يتمتع بمقدرة روحية تامة ويعلم في نفسه أنه مختار لما يفعله من الأفعال، فبالتالي وجود ذلك ووجود علمه وخبره بأنه أعلى الخاصيات التي يتميز بها كإنسان هو الدليل الآخر الذي استخدمه الماتريدي في هذا المجال.
The claimants of Islam disagreed concerning the acts of creation, the voluntary acts, into three schools. The first school is that of the Jabriyya, which says that acts in their reality are attributed to Allah, and to the servants metaphorically. As for what the Mu'tazila went to, it is the opinion that all acts of creation are in their reality attributed to the servants and their attribution to Allah is metaphorical. As for the third opinion, it is the opinion that acts in their true meanings are attributed to Allah and to the servants; and this is what Abu Mansur al-Maturidi went to and adopted. So the act according to him is attributed to Allah as creation (khalq), and to the servant as act (fi'l) and acquisition (kasb). And al-Maturidi denied the objection that says that according to the third opinion one must acknowledge the idea that the realization of the act is shared between Allah and the servant, because depicting the emergence of the act from non-existence to existence and its design in the mind with all its details, or taking that, then the act is from the capacity of the surrounding and place and limit which if one wanted to return to it, it would not be possible for the human to reach it; so the thing that is in his hand is only movement or stillness towards what is forbidden or commanded. So it is understood from the foregoing that al-Maturidi holds that an act that a human has done has many of its natural, physiological, and psychological properties, and that its agent, even if he has knowledge of some of them and that he is an agent in their realization, he nonetheless accepts that human knowledge and power have limited influence during the realization of this act in reality. And with this limited act comes its possessor to the status of the agent in its true meaning. And al-Maturidi presented there an example of a single act that in reality belongs to two, so it necessitates partnership, like two people pulling something that breaks, and two people removing something from a place; so it is seen at first glance that their act is one, but in reality they become partners through this act not in the act itself but in that it is their effect (maf'ul) in reality, i.e., in the breaking and removal. And that means that the act of each one in its true meaning is attributed to its doer; so it is not permissible for one to have the power to strengthen another in his act, nor can he create the act of another. And likewise no one can perform an act outside his space and outside his state in himself. And it is worth noting that each one possesses a complete spiritual capacity and knows in himself that he is free in choosing the acts that he does, so consequently the existence of that and his knowledge and experience that it is the highest of the properties by which he is distinguished as a human is the other evidence that al-Maturidi used in this field.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's classification of the three schools on human actions and his own position. It opens by stating that the claimants of Islam (Muslim sects) disagreed into three schools regarding voluntary acts. The first is Jabriyya, who attribute acts in reality to Allah and to servants only metaphorically. The second is Mu'tazila, who attribute acts in reality to servants and to Allah metaphorically. The third is Maturidi's own: acts are attributed to Allah as creation and to the servant as act and acquisition. Then Maturidi refutes an objection that his view implies partnership between Allah and the servant in the act. He explains that the act's coming into existence from nothingness and its detailed design are beyond human capacity; the human only has movement or stillness towards the commanded or forbidden. Thus human knowledge and power have limited influence. Then he gives an example of two people pulling a rope that breaks: it appears as one act, but each is responsible for their own part; they are partners in the effect (the breaking) not in the act itself. So each person's act is attributed to him alone; no one can empower another or create another's act, nor act outside his own scope. Finally, he adds another evidence: every human possesses a complete spiritual capacity and knows in himself that he freely chooses his actions—that inner awareness is evidence of human agency and responsibility. This supports Maturidi's kasb theory that humans are real agents without being creators.

Key Terms
جبرية (Jabriyya)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)
خلق (khalq)
فعل (fi'l)
كسب (kasb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi's kasb is a compromise that makes humans powerless puppets.
Correction: Maturidi insists on real human agency and responsibility through acquisition; the human act is truly his, though Allah creates it.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how is an act attributed to Allah and the servant?
A) To Allah as act, to servant as creation
B) To Allah as creation, to servant as act and acquisition
C) To both equally as act
D) To neither, only metaphorical

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's third school attributes acts to Allah as creation and to humans as act and acquisition, avoiding the extremes of Jabriyya and Mu'tazila.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you tend to feel that Allah created it and you had no choice, or do you take full responsibility? How does Maturidi's teaching challenge each tendency?
?
Which of the three schools do you find yourself naturally leaning towards when you reflect on your daily actions? What does that reveal about your understanding of divine decree?
?
If you truly believed that every action is both created by Allah and acquired by you as an act of real agency, how would that change the way you approach repentance and self-reform starting today?

Divine Decree
intermediate
6 min
Refuting the Charge of Partnership
Maturidi refutes the objection that his view implies shared agency, using an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's response to the objection that his view implies shared divine-human agency, including his example of two people pulling a rope.

Core Idea
Human acts are individually attributed to each agent, not jointly shared with Allah.

After presenting his position, Maturidi anticipates an objection: if acts are attributed to both Allah and the servant, then the act must be shared between them. In this lesson, Maturidi refutes this by distinguishing between the act's creation and its execution, using the vivid example of two people pulling a rope that breaks. This clarifies his doctrine of kasb.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Identify the objection and Maturidi's counter-argument.
The objection is that his view implies partnership (shirk) in the act.
2
Analyze the example
Focus on the example of two people pulling a rope. How does it illustrate that the act is not shared but individually attributed?
They are partners in the effect (breaking), not in the act.
3
Connect to kasb
Relate this to Maturidi's overall position: Allah creates the act, the human acquires it. The human is a real agent but not a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلف منتحلوا الإسلام في أفعال الخلق الأفعال الإرادية في ثلاثة مذاهب. فالمذهب الأول هو للجبرية التي تقول بأن الأفعال في حقيقتها تضاف إلى الله، وإلى العباد مجازا. وأما الذي ذهبت إليه المعتزلة هو الرأي القائل بأن أفعال الخلق كلها منسوبة في حقيقتها إلى العباد ونسبتها إلى الله مجاز. وأما الرأي الثالث فهو الرأي القائل بأن الأفعال في معانيها الحقيقية تضاف إلى الله وإلى العباد؛ وهو ما ذهب إليه وتبناه أبو منصور الماتريدي. فالفعل عنده منسوب إلى الله خلقا، وإلى العبد فعلا وكسبا. وقد أنكر الماتريدي الاعتراض القائل بأنه حسب الرأي الثالث ينبغي الاعتراف بفكرة كون تحقق الفعل مشتركا بين الله وبين العبد، لأن تصوير خروج الفعل من العدم إلى الوجود وتصميمه في الذهن بجميع تفاصيله، أو أخذ ذلك، فإن الفعل من قدر المحيط والمكان والحد الذي لو أراد أن يعود إليه لا يمكن للإنسان تلاقيه؛ فالشيء الذي في يده عبارة عن التحرك أو السكون نحو المنهي أو المأمور به. فيفهم مما تقدم أن الماتريدي يرى بأن فعلا ما قد فعله الإنسان فله كثير من خاصياته الطبيعية والفيزيولوجية والنفسية، وأن فاعله مهما كان عنده علم بالبعض منها وأنه عامل في تحققها، فهو رغم ذلك يقبل بأن علم الإنسان وقدرته لهما نفوذ محدد أثناء تحقق هذا الفعل بالواقع. ومع هذا الفعل المحدود يأتي بصاحبه إلى مكانة الفاعل بمعناه الحقيقي. وقد قدم الماتريدي هناك مثالا للفعل الواحد الذي يكون في الحقيقة لاثنين، فهو يوجب الشركة، نحو مد اثنين شيئا ينقطع، وإزالة اثنين شيئا عن مكان؛ فيرى في الوهلة الأولى أن فعلهما واحد، والحقيقة هما يصيران بهذا الفعل شريكين لا في الفعل نفسه بل في أنه مفعولهما في الحقيقة، أي في الانقطاع والإزالة. ويعني ذلك أن فعل كل واحد في معناه الحقيقي منسوب إلى فاعله؛ فلا يجوز أن يملك أحد تقوية آخر على فعله، ولا يقدر أن يخلق فعل الآخر. وكذلك لا أحد يقدر أن يفعل فعلا في غير حيزه وغير حال في نفسه. ومن الجدير بالإشارة إلى أن كل واحد يتمتع بمقدرة روحية تامة ويعلم في نفسه أنه مختار لما يفعله من الأفعال، فبالتالي وجود ذلك ووجود علمه وخبره بأنه أعلى الخاصيات التي يتميز بها كإنسان هو الدليل الآخر الذي استخدمه الماتريدي في هذا المجال.

What does Maturidi's example of two people pulling a rope illustrate?
A) That two people can share one act

Maturidi refutes the partnership objection by showing that human agency is real but limited; each act is individually attributed to its doer, not jointly shared.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your actions are your own, even though Allah creates their capacity and limits? In what areas of your life do you deny responsibility by attributing your failures to 'fate' or 'Allah's will'? Will you now own every movement and stillness as your own act?
?
When you sin, do you blame your circumstances, your upbringing, or others? How does knowing that your act is individually attributed to you alone change the way you repent and seek forgiveness? Commit to taking full accountability for one specific sin today.
?
Consider the example of two people pulling a rope. In your relationships—with family, friends, or colleagues—do you share blame for wrongs committed together, or do you honestly admit your own part? Will you, starting now, accept your personal role in conflicts and seek to repair it?

Inner Awareness as Evidence of Free Choice
Maturidi argues that inner awareness of free choice proves human agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that every human's inner awareness of free choice is evidence of real human agency and responsibility.

Core Idea
Inner knowledge of free choice proves that humans are responsible agents.

In the final part of the passage, Maturidi adds a compelling argument from human self-awareness. Every person knows within themselves that they freely choose their actions. This inner experience, he argues, is the highest proof of human agency and responsibility. This lesson explores that evidence and its role in supporting the kasb theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the final passage
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the inner awareness argument.
Notice the phrase 'يعلم في نفسه أنه مختار' – he knows in himself that he is free.
2
Reflect on the argument
Why does Maturidi consider this inner knowledge to be a strong evidence? How does it support his view over the Jabriyya?
3
Connect to daily experience
Think about your own sense of choice when you decide to do something. Does this inner feeling align with Maturidi's claim?
This self-evident experience is a key part of his proof.

Source Text

?

?

?

Divine Decree

Two Types of Capability and Their Scope
Maturidi divides capability into two types and affirms one capability for both good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's twofold division of capability and why one capability suffices for both obedience and disobedience.

Core Idea
Capability is not just physical readiness; it has a deeper dimension that accompanies the act.

This passage from Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid examines the nature of human capability (qudra). Maturidi distinguishes between the external preconditions for action and an intrinsic capacity that is inseparable from the act itself. This distinction is crucial for understanding how the same capability can be used for both good and evil, a key Sunni position against the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the full Arabic text and its literal English translation. Try to get a sense of the flow and key terms.
Pay attention to the two descriptions of capability.
2
Identify the two types of capability
Find where Maturidi defines the first type (preparatory) and the second type (accompanying the act). Notice the contrast between 'preparing the ground' and an indefinable meaning.
The first is external; the second is intrinsic.
3
Understand the role of the first type in obligation
The passage says the person is obligated (mukallaf) by the first type. Consider why this preparatory capacity is the basis for moral responsibility.
This links capability to taklif.
4
Note the agreement with Abu Hanifa
Maturidi explicitly aligns with Abu Hanifa that a single capability suffices for both obedience and disobedience. Reflect on the theological implications.
This counters the Mu'tazilite view of act-specific capability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد رأينا في الكتب الكلامية المتأخرة حول مباحث القضاء والقدر أن مسائل الاستطاعة (القدرة) والإرادة كانت موضع بحث فيها؛ فقد كانت البيانات التي قدمها الماتريدي في تلك الموضوعات مثالاً ونموذجا للتراث السني في الموضوع. فالأصل عنده في المسمى باسم القدرة التي بها يتحقق الفعل أنها على قسمين: أحدهما هي التي تجهز الأرضية لها بسلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات، والثاني هي معنى لا يقدر على تبيين حده بشيء سوى أنه ليس إلا للفعل لا يجوز وجوده بحال إلا ويقع به الفعل معه عندما يقع. فالمرء بالقسم الأول من القدرة يكون مكلفا. وقد ذهب الماتريدي مثل أبي حنيفة إلى أن القدرة الواحدة تصلح للأمرين، أي الطاعة والمعصية جميعا.
And we have seen in the later kalam books concerning the investigations of predestination and decree (qada wa qadar) that the issues of capability (istita'a/qudra) and will (irada) were subjects of discussion in them; indeed the statements that al-Maturidi presented on those topics were an example and model for the Sunni heritage on the subject. So the principle according to him concerning what is named capability (qudra) by which the act is realized is that it is of two types: one is that which prepares the ground for it by the safety of causes and soundness of instruments, and the second is a meaning that cannot be defined by anything except that it is only for the act; it is not possible for it to exist in any state except that the act occurs with it when it occurs. So the person is obligated by the first type of capability. And al-Maturidi went, like Abu Hanifa, to the view that the single capability suffices for both matters, i.e., obedience and disobedience together.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage addresses the nature of human capability (qudra). Maturidi presents a twofold division. The first type of capability is the one that prepares the ground by ensuring that the causes are sound and the instruments are healthy—this is the preconditions for action. The second type is an intrinsic meaning that cannot be further defined except that it is exclusively for the act; it does not exist without the act occurring with it. The first type is what makes the person morally obligated (mukallaf). Maturidi agrees with Abu Hanifa that a single capability (qudra wahida) suffices for both obedience and disobedience; in other words, the same capability can be used for either good or evil actions. This is a key point in Sunni theology against the Mu'tazila who held that capability only exists at the moment of acting and is specific to the act.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
استطاعة (istita'a)
تجهيز الأرضية (tajhiz al-ardiyya)
سلامة الأسباب (salamat al-asbab)
صحة الآلات (sihhat al-alat)
تكليف (taklif)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Capability is only physical ability or readiness.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes between preparatory capacity (physical readiness) and an intrinsic capacity that accompanies the act, showing capability has two levels.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the first type of capability?
A) A meaning that cannot be defined
B) That which prepares the ground by safety of causes and soundness of instruments
C) The ability to choose
D) The act itself

Key Takeaway
Maturidi divides capability into a preparatory type (basis for obligation) and an intrinsic type (inseparable from the act), and affirms that one capability serves both obedience and disobedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have been given health, limbs, and means—the preparatory capacity for action. Yet how often do you use these to delay obedience? This lesson says this capacity is the very basis of obligation. What act of worship have you been postponing that you have no real excuse for? Commit today to performing it before the day ends.
?
When you sin, do you tell yourself that you were powerless? This lesson teaches that the same single capability that enables obedience also enables disobedience. There is no separate 'weakness' for sin—only your choice. Will you take ownership of that choice and turn to Allah in repentance, using that same capacity for good?
?
The second type of capability is a mysterious meaning that only exists with the act. It means that when you step forward in obedience, Allah grants the ability at that very moment. What step of faith have you been afraid to take, fearing you lack the strength? Will you trust that the capacity will come with the action, and take that step today?

Maturidi's Refutation of Mu'tazilite Human Power
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite view that humans can act contrary to God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the Mu'tazilite claim of human power independent of God's knowledge leads to a contradiction of tawhid, equivalent to dualism.

Core Idea
Claiming humans can act outside Allah's knowledge implies another creator, refuting tawhid.

Imam Maturidi engages with the Mu'tazilite assertion that humans possess power to act contrary to what God knows. He exposes a critical flaw: if true, this would mean human acts escape God's knowledge, undermining His unique knowledge and sovereignty. Maturidi then draws a powerful analogy, equating this position with the dualist heresy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
The passage contains two distinct points of refutation.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
Locate the Mu'tazilite claim as presented: that the servant has power to perform acts outside of what God knows.
3
Trace Maturidi's logical consequence
Follow how Maturidi draws out the implication that this gives the servant a power to conceal the act from God, which would not negate God's oneness only if another god existed.
Notice the analogy: 'similarly if there were another god.'
4
Understand the unifying refutation
Reflect on Maturidi's conclusion that the very proof establishing tawhid also refutes both Mu'tazilism and dualism.
5
Note the second argument
Read the second point about the Mu'tazila affirming a power to nullify God's management and repel His threat.
This is a separate but related critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من مذهب المعتزلة أن العبد يقدر على فعل خارج مما علم الله أن يكون; إذ كل من هو في علم الله أنه يكون كافرًا يقدر على أن يكون مؤمنًا، وحقيقة كونه خروج عن علمه; فأوجب ذلك للعبد قدرة إسرار الفعل عن الله ، ثم لم ينف وحدانيته; فكذلك لو كان ثمة إله آخر . فهذا لِتَعْلم أن مذهبهم عند التحصيل مذهب الزنادقة; إذ الذي به يثبت التوحيد هو الذي ينقض المذهبين جميعا . والله الموفق.
والحرف الثاني : يثبتون للعبد قدرة في نفي جميع تدبيره من التوالد ودفع وعيده
Then from the doctrine of the Mu'tazila is that the servant has power over an act outside of what God knows to be; for everyone who is in God's knowledge that he will be a disbeliever has the power to be a believer, and the reality of that is an exit from His knowledge; so that necessitates for the servant a power of concealing the act from God, then it does not negate His oneness; similarly if there were another god. So this is for you to know that their doctrine, upon examination, is the doctrine of the zanadiqa; for that by which tawhid is established is the same that refutes both doctrines. And God is the grantor of success.
And the second point: they affirm for the servant power to negate all His management from procreation and to repel His threat.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position on human free will. Maturidi explains that the Mu'tazila claim the servant has power to perform acts that are outside (خارج) what God knows will occur. For example, someone whom God knows will be a disbeliever could choose to be a believer, which would mean the act occurs contrary to God's knowledge. This implies the servant has the ability to hide his actions from God. Maturidi argues that if this were true, it would be similar to positing another god who can act independently. In reality, the same proof that establishes tawhid (God’s unique knowledge and power) also refutes both the dualist and the Mu'tazilite positions. He then adds a second argument: the Mu'tazila attribute to the servant the power to nullify God's management in procreation and to repel His threat (i.e., avoid punishment).

Key Terms
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)
قدرة (power)
علم الله (God's knowledge)
توحيد (tawhid)
زنادقة (zanadiqa/heretics)
تدبير (management/decree)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi's critique denies human free will entirely.
Correction: Maturidi is not denying human responsibility; he is rejecting the Mu'tazilite claim that human power operates independently of God's knowledge, which would compromise tawhid.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi compare the Mu'tazilite view to the dualist position?
A) Both deny the existence of God
B) Both attribute independent power to something other than God, contradicting His unique knowledge
C) Both believe in multiple gods
D) Both reject the Quran

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that any claim of power independent of God’s knowledge ultimately contradicts the core principle of tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act as if your choices are hidden from Allah? Reflect on a time you made a decision as if His knowledge did not encompass it. How does this passage call you to repent?
?
This lesson shows that claiming independence from Allah's knowledge verges on shirk. Where in your heart do you find a desire for autonomy that resists submission to His decree? What specific step can you take today to surrender that area to Allah?
?
Maturidi states that the same proof that establishes tawhid refutes both the dualist and the Mu'tazilite. Does your belief in tawhid truly shape your daily actions, or have you compartmentalized it? Ask yourself: In what area is your life inconsistent with the oneness you profess?

Maturidi's Refutation of Mu'tazilite Free Will
Maturidi argues that Mu'tazilite free will implies zandaqah by giving the servant power over God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the Mu'tazilite view on human power logically leads to heresy by undermining God's knowledge.

Core Idea
Human power cannot override divine knowledge without implying another god.

Maturidi presents a critical refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine of free will, showing that if humans can act outside God's knowledge, then God's omniscience is compromised, and the same logic could be used to posit other gods. This passage reveals the high stakes of theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the key terms: معتزلة, قدرة, علم الله.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
The passage starts by stating the Mu'tazilite position: the servant has power over acts outside what God knows will be.
This is the thesis Maturidi will critique.
3
Analyze the example and implication
Maturidi gives the example of a disbeliever whom God knows will be a disbeliever, yet the Mu'tazila claim he can become a believer. This implies a departure from God's knowledge.
The reality of the act becomes hidden from God.
4
Follow the analogy to another god
Maturidi argues that if a human can act outside God's knowledge, then the same logic would allow for another god with hidden power.
This undermines tawhid.
5
Understand the conclusion of zandaqah
Maturidi concludes that the Mu'tazilite doctrine, when analyzed, is the doctrine of the zanadiqa because their method of proving tawhid is self-defeating.
This shows the severity of the error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من مذهب المعتزلة أن العبد يقدر على فعل خارج مما علم الله أن يكون؛ إذ كل من هو في علم الله أنه يكون كافرًا يقدر على أن يكون مؤمنًا، وحقيقة كونه خروج عن علمه؛ فأوجب ذلك للعبد قدرة إسرار الفعل عن الله، ثم لم ينف وحدانيته؛ فكذلك لو كان ثمة إله آخر. فهذا لتعلم أن مذهبهم عند التحصيل مذهب الزنادقة؛ إذ الذي به يثبت التوحيد هو الذي ينقض المذهبين جميعا. والله الموفق.
Then, from the doctrine of the Mu'tazila is that the servant has power over an act outside of what God knows to be; for everyone who is in God's knowledge that he will be a disbeliever, he has power to be a believer, and the reality of his being is a departure from His knowledge. So that necessitated for the servant a power of concealing the act from God, then it did not negate His oneness; so likewise if there were another god. This is so that you know that their doctrine at analysis is the doctrine of the zanadiqa (heretics); for that by which they affirm tawhid is that which nullifies both doctrines. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage critiques the Mu'tazilite position on free will, specifically their claim that the servant can perform an act that is outside what God knows will happen. It begins by identifying the passage as a refutation. Maturidi argues that according to the Mu'tazila, a person whom God knows will be a disbeliever can still choose to be a believer. This would make the actual state of the person a departure from God's knowledge—implying that God is ignorant. This gives the servant the power to hide his action from God, yet the Mu'tazila do not deny God's oneness. But if such power were attributed to a human, then one could also posit another god with hidden power. Thus, Maturidi concludes that Mu'tazilite doctrine essentially aligns with zandaqah (heresy) because their method of affirming tawhid is the same as that which would negate it. The passage ends with a prayer for divine success.

Key Terms

علم الله
خروج عن علم
إسرار
زنادقة
توحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi rejects all human free will.
Correction: Maturidi rejects only the Mu'tazilite notion that human power can override divine knowledge, not free will per se.

Checkpoint

A) Affirmation of God's oneness.
B) The doctrine of the zanadiqa (heretics).
C) A stronger belief in divine destiny.
D) The existence of multiple gods.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the Mu'tazilite free will thesis logically implies a dangerous inconsistency in tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever live as if your choices are hidden from Allah? Maturidi exposes the subtle danger of thinking humans can override divine knowledge. In what area of your life are you most tempted to believe that Allah's knowledge does not encompass your decisions, and what would it cost you to fully surrender to His encompassing awareness?
?
This passage calls the Mu'tazilite view heretical because it undermines tawhid. When you make plans, do you genuinely acknowledge that Allah's will and knowledge are absolute, or do you harbor a secret sense of independence? What concrete habit could you adopt to remind yourself daily that your power is merely a gift and never separate from His permission?
?
Maturidi's logic shows that a seemingly small error about human power can lead to a serious deviation. Are there any beliefs you hold about your own agency that, if pushed to their logical conclusion, could compromise your understanding of Allah's oneness and sovereignty? Will you honestly examine those beliefs today, or will you avoid the discomfort of such scrutiny?

Second Refutation: Power Over Divine Threats
Maturidi argues that Mu'tazila grant humans power to nullify God's decrees and threats.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the Mu'tazilite doctrine of human free will implies that God's decrees and threats can be nullified, which leads to unbelief.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila give humans power to undo God's plans, making God weak.

This passage is the second of Maturidi's refutations of the Mu'tazila. He argues that their notion of human freedom goes too far: according to them, humans can even negate God's explicit threats, like filling Hell with the damned. Maturidi shows that this logically undermines God's power and leads to doctrines akin to materialism and heresy.

Guided Path
1
Set the context
Recall that Maturidi is refuting the Mu'tazila. This is his second argument. Read the passage slowly to grasp its flow.
The Arabic phrase 'الحرف الثاني' marks the start.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
Maturidi states that the Mu'tazila affirm for the servant a power to negate all of God's arrangement (tadbir) and to repel His threat (waʿid). Identify what 'tadbir' and 'waʿid' refer to.
The threat is the promise to fill Hell.
3
Analyze the implication
If the servant can negate God's will, then God's will is not fulfilled. Maturidi says the Mu'tazila even claim God tries to prevent disbelief but fails. Consider how this affects God's sovereignty.
This implies God is not omnipotent.
4
See the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that such a view leads to the doctrine of the dahriyya (materialists) and zanadiqa (heretics), not Islam. Reflect on why he draws this parallel.
The Mu'tazila still affirm God, but Maturidi argues their logic contradicts that affirmation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحرف الثاني : يثبتون للعبد قدرة في نفي جميع تدبيره من التوالد وفي دفع وعيده من قوله لأملأن جهنم الآية، ويثبتون فعل جميع الكفرة والأبالسة بغير الذي يريد الله كونه؛ بل هو يريد أن لا يكون ويبذل في منع ذلك كل ما في خزائنه حتى لو أراد أن يزيد فيه شيئًا لم يقدر عليه. ثم لم يمنع القول بالإله، وقام الخلق على ما في علمه قيامه وكونه، فكذلك في أمر العالم. وهو يوضح ما أخبرتك أن مذهبهم ينتج مذهب أهل الدهر والزنادقة لا مذهب أهل الاسلام. والله الموفق.
And the second point: They affirm for the servant a power to negate all His (God's) arrangement (tadbir) concerning generation (al-tawālud) and to repel His threat (waʿīd) from His saying 'I will surely fill Hell' [the verse], and they affirm the action of all disbelievers and devils other than what God wills to be; rather, He wills that it not be and expends to prevent that all that is in His treasuries until if He wanted to increase it by something He would not be able to. Then it does not prevent the saying of God, and creation stands upon what is in His knowledge, its standing and its being, so likewise in the matter of the world. And it clarifies what I told you: that their doctrine produces the doctrine of the dahriyya (materialists) and the zanadiqa, not the doctrine of Islam. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is the second point (al-ḥarf al-thānī) in Maturidi's refutation of the Mu'tazila. He argues that their doctrine grants the servant power to nullify God's entire arrangement (tadbir), including the process of generation (al-tawālud), and even to thwart God's explicit threats, such as His promise to fill Hell. According to the Mu'tazila, disbelievers and devils act contrary to what God wills, and God expends all His efforts to prevent it but fails. Despite this, the Mu'tazila do not deny the existence of God and maintain that creation occurs according to His knowledge. Maturidi says this parallels the dualist position: it implies that God cannot fully implement His decree, leading to the doctrines of the dahriyya (materialists) and zanadiqa (heretics), not Islam. The passage ends with a prayer.

أهل الدهر
زنادقة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazilite view merely emphasizes human responsibility without affecting God's power.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if humans can nullify God's decrees and threats, then God is not truly all-powerful, which is a form of dualism leading to unbelief.

Checkpoint

A) God's threats are always fulfilled.
B) The servant can nullify God's threats, such as filling Hell.
C) God's threats are conditional on human choice.
D) The servant has no power over divine threats.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite doctrine of human free will, taken to its logical extreme, implies that God's will can be defeated, contradicting divine omnipotence and leading away from Islamic monotheism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Mu'tazila thought they were defending God's justice by giving humans free will, but Maturidi reveals they ended up attacking His power. In what area of your life might you be trying to protect God's reputation by denying His complete sovereignty? How can you submit to His decree even when it seems difficult?
?
Maturidi says that the Mu'tazilite view ultimately leads to the doctrine of materialists and heretics. If your understanding of free will leads you to think that God cannot stop evil, how does that affect your trust in His promises? What one change in your daily reliance on God can you make starting today?

Maturidi on Purpose of Creation and Human Responsibility
Maturidi explains the benefit in creation as a trial and relates righteousness to human action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's view that creation serves as a trial for humans and that righteousness or corruption stems from one's own actions.

Core Idea
Creation is a trial; your righteousness is your own doing.

Maturidi begins by affirming that every creation holds benefit for the one tested, serving as a means of inference and reflection. He then turns to human responsibility, clarifying that a servant's righteousness or corruption arises from their own action, not from divine compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
2
Identify the two main claims
Notice the first claim about creation as a trial and benefit, and the second claim about human action being the root of righteousness or corruption.
These are two distinct but connected ideas.
3
Understand the role of God's favors
Maturidi emphasizes that God provides the means for righteousness, but the individual must act. Reflect on how this balances divine grace and human effort.
4
Analyze the explanation of corruption
The passage explains that corruption results from turning away from God and preferring desires. God leaves the person to their choice.
This reinforces human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم في الجملة لا يخلو خلق من أن يكون للمتحن به نفع وعبرة من طريق الاستدلال به والاعتبار، سوى المنافع الآخر مما من الله عليهم بها. وبالله التوفيق. وأصل صلاح العبد في الدين إنما هو بفعله، وكذلك فساده. والله تعالى بالأسباب التي بها ينال فعل الصلاح عليه أعظم المنن وأجزل النعم. ومن فسد فهو لإعراض عن الله وإيثاره شهوته على طاعته خلّى الله بينه وبين ما اختار [ ] لنفسه، إذ اثر هواه على أمره وشهوته على طاعته والفعل الذي بيّن له أنه فعل العداوة على ما هو الولاية. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then in general, no creation is devoid of being for the one tried a benefit and a lesson through the way of inference and consideration, besides the other benefits which God has bestowed upon them. And with God is success. The root of the servant's righteousness in religion is only by his own action, and likewise his corruption. And God, through the means by which the action of righteousness is attained, [bestows] upon him the greatest favors and most abundant blessings. And whoever becomes corrupt, it is due to turning away from God and preferring his desire over His obedience. God leaves him and what he has chosen for himself, since he preferred his whim over His command and his desire over His obedience and the action which was made clear to him as enmity over that which is friendship. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage clarifies that all creation has a benefit for humans as a trial and a means of inference. Then Maturidi emphasizes that a person's righteousness or corruption stems from their own actions. God provides the means to achieve righteousness, but if someone becomes corrupt, it is because they turn away from God and follow their desires. God then leaves them to their own choices. This is consistent with the Maturidi doctrine of human responsibility.

Key Terms

فساد
إعراض
هوى
شهوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi teaches predestination, where all actions are forced by God.
Correction: The passage clearly states that righteousness and corruption are by the servant's own action, and corruption comes from turning away and choosing desires. This affirms human responsibility.

Checkpoint

A) God's predestination.
B) The servant's own action.
C) The intercession of prophets.
D) The environment.

Key Takeaway
Creation is a trial, and each person's righteousness or corruption stems from their own choices and actions, with God providing the means for good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
'There is no power except through God.' Do you truly live as if your ability to choose and act comes from Allah, while still owning your choices? In what situation do you most need to combine reliance on Allah with personal responsibility, and how will you do that starting today?

The Proper Response to Sophistical Questions
Describes the method for answering sophistry and applies it to the egg question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that sophistical questions are best answered by exposing contradictions or through discipline, not by providing direct rational proofs.

Core Idea
Sophistry is answered by discipline and exposing contradictions, not by proofs.

Maturidi begins by addressing a common tactic of sophists: asking meaningless questions to test God's power. He explains that such obstinate refutation (taʿannut) should be met with discipline that prevents it, not with detailed argument. He then demonstrates this by analyzing the classic egg question, showing its inherent contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on sophistry
Read the first two sentences of the Arabic passage. They describe the nature of taʿannut and the proper response.
Notice that Maturidi distinguishes between genuine inquiry and obstinate questioning.
2
Understand the egg question
Read the dialogue where someone asks if God can fit the world into an egg. Maturidi answers by pointing out the contradiction.
The contradiction is that the egg would have to be both larger and smaller than itself.
3
Reflect on the method
Consider why Maturidi does not give a direct 'yes' or 'no' but instead exposes a logical flaw.
This reflects the principle that sophistry should be countered by showing its absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سأل نفسه أشياء لا معنى للسؤال عنها إلا عن التعنّت. وحق جواب التعنّت عن التأديب بما يمنعه لا الاستدلال بالأدلة على نحو ما بينا من شأن سوفسطائي. فسأل عن أنه أليس على كل شيء قدير؟ فأجاب بـ «نعم». فقال: يقدر على إدخال الدنيا في بيضة؟ فأجاب بالتناقض، لما في ذلك جعل البيضة أوسع منها، وقد جعلها أضيق منها، إذ هي جزء منها، وكذلك هذا التأويل في الأصغر والأكبر. قال الفقيه رحمه الله : وجوابه عندنا أنه إن أراد بما قال على إبقاء البيضة بعضاً للدنيا فهو محال لما فيها انقلاب بعض كلاً، وكُلّ بعضاً بلا تغير عن حاله، وذلك تناقض. وإن أراد بالبيضة غير البيضة من الدنيا يُجعل فيها، فهو على وجهين أحدهما أن يكونا بحالها، فقد أحال لما ذكر محمد بن شبيب؛ وإن أراد بذلك تصغير ما قال أو توسيع البيض حتى يسع ما وصف فهو على ذلك قادر. وبالله التوفيق.
Then he asked himself questions whose inquiry has no meaning except as obstinate refutation. And the proper answer to obstinacy is by discipline that prevents it, not by evidence-based argument as we explained regarding the Sophists. So he asked: 'Is He not powerful over everything?' He answered: 'Yes.' Then he said: 'Can He bring the world into an egg?' He answered with contradiction, because that would make the egg larger than itself, and he has made it smaller than itself, since it is a part of it. And likewise this interpretation applies to the smaller and larger. al-Faqih (may God have mercy on him) said: Our answer is that if he meant by what he said the preservation of the egg as part of the world, then it is impossible because it would involve the transformation of a part into a whole and a whole into a part without change in its state, and that is a contradiction. And if he meant by the egg something other than the egg from the world to be placed in it, then it is two-fold: one of them is that they remain as they are, then it is impossible due to what Muhammad ibn Shabib mentioned; and if he intended by that to shrink what he said or expand the egg until it accommodates what he described, then He is able to do that. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents a classic objection: if God is all-powerful, can He fit the entire world into an egg? Maturidi first notes that such questions are obstinate (taʿannut) and deserve discipline rather than rational argument. He then answers that the question is self-contradictory because it implies making the egg both larger and smaller than itself. Al-Faqih provides a more detailed answer: if the egg remains a part of the world, then it is impossible because a part cannot become the whole without change. If the egg is taken as a separate container, then either the world stays as is (impossible due to size) or God shrinks the world or enlarges the egg, which is possible. Thus, the objection fails to disprove God's power.

Key Terms
التعنّت
سوفسطائي
تناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All questions about God's power deserve a direct rational response.
Correction: Some questions are asked sophistically and should be answered by exposing their logical flaws, not by engaging in debate.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the proper response to obstinate questions (taʿannut)?
A) Provide a detailed rational argument
B) Answer with discipline that prevents further obstinacy
C) Ignore the question entirely
D) Admit that God cannot do it

Key Takeaway
Sophistical questions about God's power are best answered by exposing their inherent contradictions, not by providing direct proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Al-Faqih's Detailed Answer to the Egg Objection
Al-Faqih analyzes three interpretations of the egg question and their implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible interpretations of the egg question and why only one is consistent with divine omnipotence.

Core Idea
The egg objection dissolves when we analyze its hidden assumptions.

Al-Faqih (the jurist) provides a more nuanced response to the egg question. He considers three distinct meanings of the question and shows that only one is logically coherent while preserving God's power. This lesson unpacks his analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Faqih's answer
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'قال الفقيه رحمه الله'. It contains three scenarios.
The scenarios depend on what is meant by 'egg' and 'world'.
2
Understand the first scenario
If the egg remains a part of the world, then fitting the world into it would require a part to become a whole, which is impossible.
This is a logical contradiction (taqāḍ).
3
Understand the second scenario
If the egg is taken as a separate container, then the world cannot fit without changing size, which is impossible if both remain as they are.
This scenario also leads to impossibility.
4
Understand the third scenario
If God shrinks the world or enlarges the egg, then it is possible, because God can do that.
This is the only scenario that preserves omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سأل نفسه أشياء لا معنى للسؤال عنها إلا عن التعنّت. وحق جواب التعنّت عن التأديب بما يمنعه لا الاستدلال بالأدلة على نحو ما بينا من شأن سوفسطائي. فسأل عن أنه أليس على كل شيء قدير؟ فأجاب بـ «نعم». فقال: يقدر على إدخال الدنيا في بيضة؟ فأجاب بالتناقض، لما في ذلك جعل البيضة أوسع منها، وقد جعلها أضيق منها، إذ هي جزء منها، وكذلك هذا التأويل في الأصغر والأكبر. قال الفقيه رحمه الله : وجوابه عندنا أنه إن أراد بما قال على إبقاء البيضة بعضاً للدنيا فهو محال لما فيها انقلاب بعض كلاً، وكُلّ بعضاً بلا تغير عن حاله، وذلك تناقض. وإن أراد بالبيضة غير البيضة من الدنيا يُجعل فيها، فهو على وجهين أحدهما أن يكونا بحالها، فقد أحال لما ذكر محمد بن شبيب؛ وإن أراد بذلك تصغير ما قال أو توسيع البيض حتى يسع ما وصف فهو على ذلك قادر. وبالله التوفيق.
Then he asked himself questions whose inquiry has no meaning except as obstinate refutation. And the proper answer to obstinacy is by discipline that prevents it, not by evidence-based argument as we explained regarding the Sophists. So he asked: 'Is He not powerful over everything?' He answered: 'Yes.' Then he said: 'Can He bring the world into an egg?' And likewise this interpretation applies to the smaller and larger. al-Faqih (may God have mercy on him) said: Our answer is that if he meant by what he said the preservation of the egg as part of the world, then it is impossible because it would involve the transformation of a part into a whole and a whole into a part without change in its state, and that is a contradiction. And with God is success.

Commentary
This passage presents a classic objection: if God is all-powerful, can He fit the entire world into an egg? He then answers that the question is self-contradictory because it implies making the egg both larger and smaller than itself. ?

Refuting Mu'tazila on God's Power and the Impossibility of Creating Like Him
Critique of Mu'tazila and answer about creating like God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the Mu'tazila limitation on God's power and why the question of God creating a being like Himself is logically impossible.

Core Idea
God's power is unlimited in the realm of possibility; creating a like of Himself is a logical contradiction.

This passage from Maturidi addresses two related challenges: the Mu'tazila's claim that God cannot create small things, and the hypothetical question of whether God can create a being exactly like Himself. Maturidi shows both to be flawed reasoning, grounding his response in the nature of possibility and logical consistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the structure: a critique of the Mu'tazila followed by a question-and-answer about creating like God.
The Arabic text cuts off at the end, but the intended meaning is clear.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila position
The passage begins by describing the Mu'tazila claim that God cannot create the act of a mosquito or even larger atoms. Note how Maturidi criticizes this as a contradiction to their claim that God is powerful over everything.
This part shows the incoherence of limiting God's power to only 'big' things.
3
Understand the response about possibility
Maturidi argues that objecting based on things outside the realm of possibility (like creating a being like God) is meaningless. Focus on the phrase 'outside the limit of possibility in the intellects'.
This sets up the second part of the passage.
4
Analyze the answer about creating like Himself
Read Maturidi's response to his own question: God cannot create a being exactly like Himself because it would necessitate a creature (makhluq). Understand why this is a logical impossibility, not a limitation of power.
The key point: 'creating like Himself' would mean the created being is both God and creature, a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصاحب الكتاب ينتحل نخلة الاعتزال، ومذهبهم أن الله لا يقدر على خلق فعل بعوض فما فوقه من الجواهر، وفعل ذلك كله واقع تحت القدرة أو في ذلك لغيره قدرة. فأبوا تحقيق ما ادعوا من أنه قادر على كل شيء في أكثر الأشياء التي هي في حد الإمكان في العقول. فمعارضة أمثالهم بالخارج عن حد الإمكان في العقول لا معنى له. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم سأل نفسه: يقدر أن يخلق مثله؟ قال: ذا محال، لما فيه إيجاب مخلوق، والمخلوق
And the author of the book adopts the palm tree of Mu'tazila, and their doctrine is that God is not able to create the act of a mosquito or what is above it from atoms, and all of that act occurs under power or in that there is power for other than Him. So they refused to realize what they claimed—that He is powerful over everything—in most things that are within the limit of possibility in the intellects. So opposing their likes with what is outside the limit of possibility in the intellects has no meaning. And there is no power except with God. Then he asked himself: Is He able to create the like of Himself? He said: That is impossible, because it would necessitate a creature, and the creature...
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage criticizes the Mu'tazila (whom the author equates with a particular stance) for holding that God cannot create certain things like small actions of a mosquito, thus contradicting their claim that He is powerful over everything. Maturidi argues that such objections are based on impossible scenarios (outside the realm of possibility), so they are irrelevant. Then he raises another question: Can God create a being exactly like Himself? The answer is that this is impossible because it would entail making a creature equal to the Creator, which is a logical contradiction (the created would be a creature, not God). The passage ends abruptly, but the intended answer is clear.

Key Terms

قدرة
الإمكان
مخلوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that asking 'Can God create a being like Himself?' is a valid test of omnipotence, and that if God cannot, then He is not all-powerful.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this question is logically impossible—it would entail a creature being the Creator. God's power extends only to what is possible; logical contradictions are not within the realm of possibility.

Checkpoint

A) Yes, God can do anything
B) No, because it would necessitate a creature, which is impossible
C) He refuses to answer
D) It is possible but God does not do it

Key Takeaway
Maturidi asserts that God's power encompasses all that is logically possible, and the question of creating a being like Himself is invalid because it involves a logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Examine a current difficulty you face and identify a 'logical' excuse you've made for why God cannot resolve it. Then surrender that excuse to Him.
?
When you pray, do you subconsciously exclude certain requests because you think they are 'logically impossible'? How does this lesson challenge you to pray more boldly, trusting that God's power covers all that is possible in His wisdom?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed God could not create a mosquito's act—a small thing. What small, seemingly insignificant matter have you stopped relying on God for, thinking He is not concerned or able? Repent of that limitation now and place your trust in His infinite power.
