جعل
شيئية
كفر
إيمان
خالق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God does not create the quiddity of actions, then He cannot punish or reward them, or that this leads to associating partners with God.
Correction: Maturidi argues that none of these consequences follow: punishment is for the action itself, not the quiddity, and shirk is avoided because quiddity is not a separate agent.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which of the following does NOT follow from the Mu'tazilite position on kufr and iman?
A) That God punishes for nothing
B) That God punishes for the quiddity itself
C) That the act can be attributed to two agents
D) That the servant is the sole creator of his actions

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the Mu'tazilite separation of quiddity from existence does not lead to absurd consequences, and the act remains attributable to the servant for reward and punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the Mu'tazilite-Maturidi debate about the quiddity of actions affect your own understanding of your sins? If the very 'thing-ness' of a sin is not created by you, why do you deserve punishment? Read the passage again and ask yourself: Do I truly believe that every sin I commit is ultimately created by God, yet I am fully accountable? How does that change the way you feel about your past mistakes and seek forgiveness?
?
Maturidi uses the analogy of a paralyzed person's movement: the quiddity is absent yet the movement is still attributed. Think of a situation where you felt powerless over a wrong action you committed. In light of this lesson, what does that say about your responsibility? Are there areas in your life where you have denied ownership of your actions, blaming circumstance or your nature, while God's creation of the act remains? Commit today to accept full moral responsibility for one specific action you have been excusing.
?
The Mu'tazilites fear that attributing the quiddity of sin to God would make Him unjust. Maturidi responds that God's punishment is not for the quiddity but for the act as a whole. Ask yourself: Do you secretly doubt God's justice when you see evil in the world? How does this lesson's reasoning—that the quiddity is not the ground for punishment—help you trust that God's judgment is perfectly just, even when you cannot see how? Write down one doubt you have about divine justice and then read the source translation again to find Maturidi's counterargument.

Divine Decree
intermediate
5 min
Refuting al-Ka'bi on the Basis of Blame
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi by showing that blame based on sin would necessitate blaming all actions, including good ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attaching blame to the aspect of sin leads to absurd consequences, and how Maturidi uses this to defend divine justice.

Core Idea
Blame cannot be attached to sin without also blaming faith and goodness.

This lesson begins with al-Ka'bi's claim that the doer of sin is more blameworthy than its Creator. Maturidi responds with a powerful logical counter: if blame were based on the aspect of sinfulness, then every act sharing that aspect would be blameworthy, including faith and virtue. This refutation sets the stage for a deeper understanding of how acts relate to God and human beings.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Ka'bi's claim and Maturidi's response
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the structure: a claim followed by a rhetorical question.
2
Identify the absurd consequence
Maturidi argues that if blame were tied to the aspect of sin, then all acts would be blameworthy, including faith. Find this part in the text.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the logic
Why cannot blame be absolute? Consider that blame must be directed appropriately. The passage concludes that aspects must be assigned to what fits them.
4
Connect to the larger argument
This refutation clears the way for Maturidi's dual-aspect theory. It shows that blame is not inherent in the act's creation but in its relation to the servant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال الكعبي : ما جعل فاعل المعصية أحق بالذم من خالقها . قيل له : وما جعل جهة المعصية أحق بالذم من جهة الشيئية والحركة والحدثية والعرضية؛ وأنه خلافٌ للعبد والله وغير لهما، وأنه حجة الله ودليل سفه الكافر؛ فإن الذمّ لشيء من ذلك لزمه الذم بكل مسمّى به؛ فيجب الذم على فعل الإيمان وكل حسن، وإن لم يجب ثبت لذلك جهات، [ف] يصرف إلى كل ما يليق به. ثم الذي من الله تعالى حكمة من حيث جعلُه في الحقيقة قبيحا وسفها وجورًا ومذموماً، وهو من هذا الوجه حق وحكمة، والفعل من حيث العبد سفه وجور، ومن ذلك الوجه قبيح ومعصية. ألا ترى أن من عرف فعل الكافر على ما هو عنده كان جاهلاً ومن أخبر به كان كاذبًا، ومن عرفه على ما عليه حقيقته كان عالما حكيمًا، ولو أخبر به كان صادقا. فعلى ذلك خلق الله ذلك وجعله على ماهو عليه فعل العبد. وعلى قول من يجعل خلق الشيء غيره لا معنى له، لأن فعل الله في الحقيقة ليس بكفر ولا جور ولا سفه ولا الذي كان من العبد من خضوع وذلة وطاعة ومعصية. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم نعارض بتسميته غير الذي خلق الموت وأحوال الخلق أحق من الذي خلق، وهو بالجملة مُسمّى أنه خالق ذلك. فمهما قال في ذلك فهو جواب له في الأول.
Then al-Ka'bi said: 'What makes the doer of sin more deserving of blame than its Creator?' It was said to him: 'And what makes the aspect of sin more deserving of blame than the aspects of quiddity, movement, origination, and accident? And that it is contrary to the servant and God and other than them both, and that it is God's proof and evidence of the disbeliever's folly, for blame for any of that would necessitate blame for every named thing; then blame would be obligatory for the act of faith and every good act, and if it is not obligatory, then aspects are established for it, so it is directed to whatever is appropriate to it. Then that which is from God Almighty is wisdom in terms of His making it in reality ugly, folly, injustice, and blameworthy, yet in this respect it is truth and wisdom; and the act from the servant's perspective is folly and injustice, and in that respect it is ugly and disobedience. Do you not see that whoever knows the act of the disbeliever as it is according to himself is ignorant, and whoever reports it is a liar, and whoever knows it as it truly is is wise, and if he reports it he is truthful. Thus God created that and made it as it is, the act of the servant. And according to the statement of one who makes creation of something other than it, it has no meaning, because the act of God in reality is not disbelief, nor injustice, nor folly, nor that which was from the servant of humility, lowliness, obedience, and disobedience. And there is no power except with God. Then we counter by naming other than Him as the one who created death and the states of creation—more deserving than the one who created, and in summary, He is named the Creator of that. So whatever he says about that, it is his answer for the first.'
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ka'bi's statement that the doer of sin is more deserving of blame than its Creator (God). Maturidi responds by questioning why the aspect of sin should be more blameworthy than other aspects like quiddity, movement, etc. He argues that if blame were attached to these aspects, then it would also apply to faith and good deeds, which is absurd. He then explains the dual aspect: from God, the act is created as an instance of wisdom and justice, even though it is ugly and blameworthy in itself; from the servant, it is folly and sin. The example of the disbeliever's act shows that knowing it as it truly is requires recognizing both aspects. God creates the act as it is—the act of the servant—and God's act in reality is not kufr or injustice. Finally, a counter-argument is presented: if one claims that something other than God is the creator of death and states, then that other would be more deserving of being called creator, but in summary God is called the Creator of that. Whatever al-Ka'bi says in response will be his own answer to the first objection.

Key Terms
معصية
ذم
خالق
فاعل
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would happen if blame were attached to the aspect of sin?
A) Only sinful acts would be blameworthy.
B) Blame would apply to every named thing, including faith.
C) God would be directly blameworthy.
D) Only the servant would be blameworthy.

Key Takeaway
Blame cannot be based on the aspect of sin without leading to absurdity; therefore, the blameworthiness of sin must be relative to the human agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever find yourself blaming your circumstances, your environment, or even God for it? How does this lesson's distinction between creation and acquisition force you to take full responsibility?
?
If you truly believed that the aspect of sin in an act comes only from your own acquisition and not from God's creative will, how would your repentance and resolve to avoid sin change starting today?
?
Consider the absurdity Maturidi exposes: if blame were based on the simple presence of sin as a quality, even faith would be blameworthy. Does your heart ever secretly feel that God is somehow responsible for your sins? What would it mean for your relationship with Him to let go of that blame entirely?

The Dual Aspect of Actions: Wisdom and Sin
Maturidi explains the dual aspect of actions and illustrates with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's dual-aspect theory: that the same act is wisdom from God's perspective and sin from the human perspective.

Core Idea
An act can be both God's wise creation and human sin.

Having refuted the simplistic blame attribution, Maturidi now introduces a nuanced framework: every action has two aspects—one relating to God as Creator, the other to the human as agent. From God, the act is wisdom and truth; from the human, it may be folly and disobedience. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications for understanding divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the dual-aspect statement
Read the first part of the passage where Maturidi states that from God the act is wisdom, and from the servant it is folly and sin.
2
Analyze the example of the disbeliever's act
Maturidi gives an example: knowing the act as the disbeliever sees it is ignorance; true knowledge sees it as it truly is. Reflect on what this illustrates.
3
Understand God's act of creation
Maturidi clarifies that God's creation of the act is not itself kufr or injustice; it is the servant's act that carries those qualities.
4
Examine the counter-argument
The passage ends with a counter: if someone says something else creates death, that other would be more deserving of the name 'creator,' but in summary, God is the Creator of all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال الكعبي : ما جعل فاعل المعصية أحق بالذم من خالقها . قيل له : وما جعل جهة المعصية أحق بالذم من جهة الشيئية والحركة والحدثية والعرضية؛ وأنه خلافٌ للعبد والله وغير لهما، وأنه حجة الله ودليل سفه الكافر؛ فإن الذمّ لشيء من ذلك لزمه الذم بكل مسمّى به؛ فيجب الذم على فعل الإيمان وكل حسن، وإن لم يجب ثبت لذلك جهات، [ف] يصرف إلى كل ما يليق به. ثم الذي من الله تعالى حكمة من حيث جعلُه في الحقيقة قبيحا وسفها وجورًا ومذموماً، وهو من هذا الوجه حق وحكمة، والفعل من حيث العبد سفه وجور، ومن ذلك الوجه قبيح ومعصية. ألا ترى أن من عرف فعل الكافر على ما هو عنده كان جاهلاً ومن أخبر به كان كاذبًا، ومن عرفه على ما عليه حقيقته كان عالما حكيمًا، ولو أخبر به كان صادقا. فعلى ذلك خلق الله ذلك وجعله على ماهو عليه فعل العبد. وعلى قول من يجعل خلق الشيء غيره لا معنى له، لأن فعل الله في الحقيقة ليس بكفر ولا جور ولا سفه ولا الذي كان من العبد من خضوع وذلة وطاعة ومعصية. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم نعارض بتسميته غير الذي خلق الموت وأحوال الخلق أحق من الذي خلق، وهو بالجملة مُسمّى أنه خالق ذلك. فمهما قال في ذلك فهو جواب له في الأول.
Then al-Ka'bi said: 'What makes the doer of sin more deserving of blame than its Creator?' It was said to him: 'And what makes the aspect of sin more deserving of blame than the aspects of quiddity, movement, origination, and accident? And that it is contrary to the servant and God and other than them both, and that it is God's proof and evidence of the disbeliever's folly, for blame for any of that would necessitate blame for every named thing; then blame would be obligatory for the act of faith and every good act, and if it is not obligatory, then aspects are established for it, so it is directed to whatever is appropriate to it. Then that which is from God Almighty is wisdom in terms of His making it in reality ugly, folly, injustice, and blameworthy, yet in this respect it is truth and wisdom; and the act from the servant's perspective is folly and injustice, and in that respect it is ugly and disobedience. Do you not see that whoever knows the act of the disbeliever as it is according to himself is ignorant, and whoever reports it is a liar, and whoever knows it as it truly is is wise, and if he reports it he is truthful. Thus God created that and made it as it is, the act of the servant. And according to the statement of one who makes creation of something other than it, it has no meaning, because the act of God in reality is not disbelief, nor injustice, nor folly, nor that which was from the servant of humility, lowliness, obedience, and disobedience. And there is no power except with God. Then we counter by naming other than Him as the one who created death and the states of creation—more deserving than the one who created, and in summary, He is named the Creator of that. So whatever he says about that, it is his answer for the first.'

Commentary

Misconception: One might think that if God creates the sin, then He is to blame.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes between the act as creation (from God) and as acquisition (from the servant), each with its own moral aspect. God's act of creation is wisdom, not sin.

Key Takeaway
The same act is wisdom from God and sin from the human; blame attaches to the human's acquisition, not to God's creation.

?

The Thing-ness of Human Actions: Divine Creation and Human Accountability
Maturidi explains that thing-ness of actions is from God, not invalidating accountability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi teaches that the 'thing-ness' of human actions (their existence as created entities) belongs to God, and how this does not undermine human accountability or lead to theological errors.

Core Idea
The existence of an action is from God; its moral quality is from man.

Imam Maturidi addresses a subtle but crucial point in Qadar: the distinction between the mere existence of an action and its moral attribution. He argues that the thing-ness (شيئية) of an action—its character as something real—is created by God, not the servant. This might seem to raise questions about punishment and shirk. In this passage, Maturidi clarifies that this view actually preserves both divine sovereignty and human responsibility, using the example of a paralyzed person's movement to illustrate.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to get the rhythm of Maturidi's argument.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice the contrast between 'شيئية' (thing-ness) and the moral attributes of the action (faith/disbelief). Maturidi assigns thing-ness to God and moral quality to the servant.
3
List the denied implications
Maturidi denies five consequences of his view: arbitrary punishment, punishment for thing-ness, impossibility of punishment if thing-ness removed, shirk between agent and thing-ness, and dual agency. Write them down.
These denials show what his view does NOT entail.
4
Reflect on the paralyzed person analogy
Consider how the movement of a paralyzed person illustrates that the thing-ness of an action is from God, but the moral responsibility (if any) attaches to the servant's intention.
This analogy helps distinguish between creation and acquisition.
5
Connect to the learning objective
Summarize how this teaching protects both divine omnipotence and human accountability without falling into error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا جعلُها شيئين، فصارا من حيث الشيئية ليس للعبد. ثم لا ننكر ذلك، فما ننكر أن يكون من حيث الشيئية خلقا، ولا يُدفع ذلك. ولم يوجب بذلك أنه عذب لا لشيء، ولا أنه عذب للشيئية، ولا أحيل التعذيب إذا سقطت عنه الشيئية، ولا أَوْجب الشرك بين الفاعل والشيئية في العقل [و] في الوجود، ولا أطلق القول بأنه لاثنين؛ إذ هو بكليته في [حد ذاته] أنه شيء ليس له، وفي أنه إيمان وكفر له. وكذا هذا التقرير في حركة المفلوج. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
He did not make them two things, so they became from the aspect of thing-ness not belonging to the servant. Then we do not deny that, so we do not deny that from the aspect of thing-ness it is creation, and that is not repelled. And that does not necessitate that He punishes for no reason, nor that He punishes for thing-ness, nor is punishment impossible if thing-ness is removed from it, nor does it necessitate association between the agent and thing-ness in intellect and existence, nor does it release the statement that it is from two; for it is in its entirety in itself that it is a thing not belonging to him, and that it is faith and disbelief belonging to him. And likewise this explanation in the movement of the paralyzed. And there is no power but with God.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is a theological explanation distinguishing between the aspect of 'thing-ness' (شيئية) of an action and the action itself as attributed to the human agent. Maturidi argues that the thing-ness of an action—its existence as a created entity—is from God, not from the servant. However, this does not lead to problematic conclusions: it does not mean that God punishes for no reason or that punishment is invalid if thing-ness is removed. It also does not imply polytheism (شرك) by having two creators. The action in its entirety is a thing not belonging to the servant in terms of its creation, yet it is faith or disbelief belonging to the servant in terms of acquisition. Maturidi gives the example of the movement of a paralyzed person to illustrate that the thing-ness of the movement is from God, but the moral responsibility attaches to the servant's intention or choice.

Key Terms
شيئية
فعل العبد
خلق
تعذيب
شرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates the existence of human actions, then man cannot be held accountable, and punishment becomes arbitrary or impossible.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the thing-ness (existence) of an action is from God, but the action's moral quality (faith or disbelief) is acquired by the servant, so accountability remains. Punishment is for the moral quality, not for the existence, and it is not arbitrary.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, to whom does the 'thing-ness' (شيئية) of an action belong?
A) The human agent exclusively
B) God
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The thing-ness (existence) of every action is created by God alone, but the action's moral character (faith/disbelief) is acquired by the servant, preserving both divine unity and human accountability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever feel that God 'created' your sin, and thus part of its blame belongs to Him? How does Maturidi's distinction between thing-ness and moral quality confront that excuse? Will you now take full responsibility for your actions, acknowledging that their existence is from God but their moral quality is your acquisition?
?
Your faith and disbelief are described as 'belonging to you' in this passage. Are you actively cultivating your faith as your own, or do you passively assume it is from God alone? What specific step will you take today to own your faith—not just as a created thing, but as a personal act of submission?
?
The paralyzed person's movement is used as an example. Think of a situation where you felt powerless over your actions—a habit, an addiction, a recurring sin. How does knowing that the 'thing-ness' of that action is from God, yet the moral quality is yours, change your approach to overcoming it? What will you do now that you cannot blame your circumstances?

Refutation of al-Ka'bi on Blame for Sin
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's claim that the doer of sin is more blamable than its creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that blame for sin falls on the human's moral choice, not on God's creation of the act.

Core Idea
Blame attaches to the human aspect of sin, not to God's act of creation.

Al-Ka'bi raised a theological challenge: if God creates every act, including sins, why is the human doer more blamable than God? Maturidi responds by distinguishing between the aspect of creation (which is from God and wise) and the aspect of moral quality (which belongs to the human). This lesson unpacks that nuanced response and its implications for divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection by al-Ka'bi
Start by reading the first line where al-Ka'bi questions why the doer is more blamable than the creator. Then read the full passage and its translation to get the complete argument.
2
Identify the response's key distinction
Notice how the response shifts from blame on the act itself to blame on the 'aspect' (جهة) of sin belonging to the human. Maturidi argues that if blame were on creation, even good acts would be blameworthy.
Look for the phrase 'جهة المعصية'.
3
Analyze the example about knowledge
Study the example comparing the ignorant disbeliever and the wise knower. Maturidi uses this to show that the same act can be seen differently depending on the aspect considered.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that God's creation of the act is not itself disbelief or injustice; those qualities come only from the human's acquisition. This refutes al-Ka'bi's claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال الكعبي: ما جعل فاعل المعصية أحق بالذم من خالقها. قيل له: وما جعل جهة المعصية أحق بالذم من جهة الشيئية والحركة والحدثية والعرضية؛ وأنه خلافٌ للعبد والله وغير لهما، وأنه حجة الله ودليل سفه الكافر؛ فإن الذمّ لشيء من ذلك لزمه الذم بكل مسمّى به؛ فيجب الذم على فعل الإيمان وكل حسن، وإن لم يجب ثبت لذلك جهات، [ف] يصرف إلى كل ما يليق به. ثم الذي من الله تعالى حكمة من حيث جعلُه في الحقيقة قبيحا وسفها وجورًا ومذموماً، وهو من هذا الوجه حق وحكمة، والفعل من حيث العبد سفه وجور، ومن ذلك الوجه قبيح ومعصية. ألا ترى أن من عرف فعل الكافر على ما هو عنده كان جاهلاً ومن أخبر به كان كاذبًا، ومن عرفه على ما عليه حقيقته كان عالما حكيمًا، ولو أخبر به كان صادقا. فعلى ذلك خلق الله ذلك وجعله على ماهو عليه فعل العبد. وعلى قول من يجعل خلق الشيء غيره لا معنى له، لأن فعل الله في الحقيقة ليس بكفر ولا جور ولا سفه ولا الذي كان من العبد من خضوع وذلة وطاعة ومعصية. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then al-Ka'bi said: What makes the doer of sin more deserving of blame than its creator? It was said to him: And what makes the aspect of sin more deserving of blame than the aspect of thing-ness, movement, event-ness, and accident-ness? And that it is different for the servant and God and other than them both, and that it is God's proof and evidence of the ignorance of the disbeliever? For blame for something from that would necessitate blame for every name by it; thus blame would be necessary upon the act of faith and every good, and if it is not necessary, then aspects are established for it, so it is directed to everything befitting it. Then that which is from God, the Exalted, is wisdom from the aspect of His making it in reality ugly, foolish, unjust, and blameworthy, and from this aspect it is true and wisdom, and the act from the aspect of the servant is foolish and unjust, and from that aspect it is ugly and disobedience. Do you not see that whoever knows the act of the disbeliever as it is according to him is ignorant, and whoever informs of it is a liar, and whoever knows it as it is in its reality is knowledgeable and wise, and if he informed of it he would be truthful. Thus, God created that and made it as it is the act of the servant. And according to the saying of one who makes the creation of a thing other than it, it has no meaning, because the act of God in reality is not disbelief, nor injustice, nor foolishness, nor that which was from the servant of humility, lowliness, obedience, and disobedience. And there is no power but with God.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by al-Ka'bi and the response. Al-Ka'bi questions why the human doer of sin is more blamable than God who creates the sin. The response argues that blame attaches to the specific aspect of the act that belongs to the human—the moral quality—not to the aspect of creation (thing-ness, movement, etc.). If blame were attached to the created aspect, then even faith and good deeds would be blameworthy, which is absurd. Instead, aspects are distinguished: from God's perspective, the creation of the act is wise and just, even though the act itself is ugly from the human perspective. The response uses the example of knowledge: knowing the act as the disbeliever does leads to ignorance, but knowing it as it truly is leads to wisdom. Thus, God's creation of the act does not make Him blameworthy; the blame falls on the human for his evil choice.

Key Terms
الذم
فاعل المعصية
خالق
جهة
خلق الشيء
حكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The doer of sin is more blamable because God created the sin directly, so He shares blame.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that blame attaches to the aspect of the act that belongs to the human's choice, not to God's act of creation. God's creation is wise and just, and the moral quality is acquired by the human.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's response, why is the doer of sin more blamable than God?
A) Because God creates the sin directly
B) Because blame attaches to the human aspect of the act, not the divine creation
C) Because God is not involved in the act
D) Because the human's will is completely independent

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi by showing that blame for sin belongs to the human's moral aspect, not to God's creation of the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever find yourself subtly blaming circumstances or even God’s decree as an excuse? How does this lesson force you to take full ownership of your sin as your own 'foolishness and injustice'? What would change in your repentance if you truly believed that?
?
If you accept that God’s creation of your actions is wise and just, but your acquisition of them is blameworthy, does that make you more careful about what you 'acquire'? Are there sins you treat as small that, according to this lesson, are entirely your responsibility?
?
Do you sometimes feel resentment toward God for allowing evil or sin? How does Maturidi's distinction between God's aspect and the human aspect shatter that resentment? Can you honestly say you now see God's role in your sin as free of blame? What would it mean to live with that conviction?

The Reality of Human Action and Divine Creation
Maturidi argues for the reality of human choice alongside divine creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that human actions are real and chosen, and that divine creation does not negate human freedom or moral accountability.

Core Idea
God's creation of actions does not compel the human; choice remains real.

Imam Maturidi addresses a key question of Islamic theology: if God creates everything, including human actions, how can humans be held responsible? In this passage, he establishes that human actions are genuine and chosen, and that God's creation, knowledge, and decree coexist with human freedom. This principle ensures the validity of divine command, prohibition, and reward.

Guided Path
1
Read and translate the passage
Read the Arabic source and the English translation carefully to grasp the overall argument. Notice the flow from objection to principle to implications.
The passage starts with a counterargument structure, so pay attention to 'Then we counter...'.
2
Identify the core principle
Locate where Maturidi states that an act is established for the servant in reality and that he has choice. This is the anchor of the entire discussion.
Look for the phrase 'an act has been established for the servant in reality, and that he has choice regarding it'.
3
Analyze the relationship with divine creation
Understand how Maturidi explains that divine creation does not push, force, or compel the human. See the list of divine actions (knowledge, informing, writing in the Preserved Tablet) that coexist without compulsion.
The phrase 'since He did not compel him to his act nor force him to it' is key.
4
Apply to moral accountability
Reflect on the conclusion: because the human is not compelled, command, prohibition, punishment, and reward are all good and consistent. This shows that divine decree and human responsibility are compatible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نعارض بتسميته غير الذي خلق الموت وأحوال الخلق أحق من الذي خلق، وهو بالجملة مُسمّى أنه خالق ذلك. فمهما قال في ذلك فهو جواب له في الأول. والأصل أنه قد ثبت للعبد فعل في الحقيقة، وأنه له مختار، وأنه أَثَر الأشياء عنده وأحبها، وأن خلق ذلك لم يدفعه إليه، ولم يحمله ولم يضطره إليه. فوجود ذلك ووجود علمه به وخبره عنه وإثباته في اللوح المحفوظ وإيجاب معاودته لوقت فعله وتسميته بما سمّى ـ إذ لم يضطره إلى فعله ولا حمله عليه ـ حسن معه الأمر والنهي والتعذيب والإثابة. ومن أنكر بهذا خلقه فتعلقه بهذا النوع خيال. وحقه أن ينظر في الوجه الذي به يُعرف خلق الأشياء، فإن أمكن تحقيقه فالإنكار بهذا النوع إنما هو جهل بالحكمة. وعلى ذلك كان أول ما جبل عليه. فسيعلم إن خضع للمكرم به إن شاء الله، وإن لم يمكن تسقط المسألة، ويُفصل الذي عارض به كلُّه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then we counter with His naming the one who created death and the states of creation is more deserving than the one who created, and in summary he is named that he is the creator of that. So whatever he says about that, it is an answer to him in the first. And the principle is that an act has been established for the servant in reality, and that he has choice regarding it, and that he is the effect of things according to him and their most beloved, and that the creation of that did not push it to him, nor did it force him, nor did it compel him to it. So the existence of that and the existence of His knowledge of it and His inform about it and His establishing it in the Preserved Tablet and necessitating its recurrence at the time of its doing and His naming it as He named—since He did not compel him to his act nor force him to it—is good along with command, prohibition, punishment, and reward. And whoever denies this creation [by God] and attaches himself to this type [of argument] is delusion. And his duty is to look into the aspect by which the creation of things is known; if its verification is possible, then denial of this type is only ignorance of wisdom. And upon that was the first thing he was created upon. So he will know—if he submits to the one honored by it, God willing—and if it is not possible, the question falls, and everything he objected with is set aside. And there is no power but with God.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage establishes the principle that human actions are real and the human has genuine choice (اختيار). God's creation of the act does not remove the human's agency; rather, God's knowledge, decree, and writing in the Preserved Tablet coexist with human freedom because God does not compel the human. Therefore, commanding, prohibiting, punishing, and rewarding are all consistent. Maturidi warns that denying this relationship is delusion and ignorance of wisdom. The opponent's objections are dismissed as not based on proper understanding of divine creation.

Correction: Maturidi explains that God's creation does not compel or force humans to act; the human still has genuine choice and is responsible.

Human Action, Divine Decree, and Moral Responsibility
Maturidi establishes the reality of human action and its compatibility with divine command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi reconciles genuine human choice with divine creation and command.

Core Idea
Human actions are real and chosen, yet created by God without compulsion.

This passage from Imam Maturidi addresses a central question in Islamic theology: How can humans be held accountable if God creates all actions? Maturidi establishes that the servant has genuine choice and real action, and that God's creation of that action does not imply compulsion. This makes divine commands and punishments meaningful.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the dense argument style of Maturidi.
The phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله' emphasizes reliance on God.
2
Identify the key claims
Look for claims: the servant has real action (fi‘l), choice (mukhtar), and that God's creation did not compel him. Note how these are presented as established principles.
3
Understand the link to divine command
The passage ties the reality of human choice to the appropriateness of command, prohibition, punishment, and reward. See how the Preserved Tablet fits in.
The phrase 'حسن معه الأمر والنهي' shows the conclusion.
4
Reflect on the objection and conclusion
Maturidi counters those who deny this, calling it illusion or ignorance of wisdom. The closing 'فسيعلم إن خضع' suggests submission leads to understanding.
Consider the role of 'اللوح المحفوظ' as record of actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل أنه قد ثبت للعبد فعل في الحقيقة، وأنه له مختار، وأنه آثر الأشياء عنده وأحبها، وأن خلق ذلك لم يدفعه إليه، ولم يحمله ولم يضطره إليه . فوجود ذلك ووجود علمه به وخبره عنه وإثباته في اللوح المحفوظ وإيجاب معاودته لوقت فعله وتسميته بما سمّى ـ إذ لم يضطره إلى فعله ولا حمله عليه ـ حسن معه الأمر والنهي والتعذيب والإثابة. ومن أنكر بهذا خلقه فتعلقه بهذا النوع خيال. وحقه أن ينظر في الوجه الذي به يُعرف خلق الأشياء، فإن أمكن تحقيقه فالإنكار بهذا النوع إنما هو جهل بالحكمة. وعلى ذلك كان أول ما جبل عليه . فسيعلم إن خضع للمكرم به إن شاء الله ، وإن لم يمكن تسقط المسألة، ويُفصل الذي عارض به كلُّه. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And the principle is that it has been established for the servant a real action, and that he has choice, and that he prefers things in his view and loves them, and that the creation of that did not push him to it, nor compel him, nor force him. So the existence of that [action] and the existence of His knowledge of it and His report about it and its inscription in the Preserved Tablet and the obligation of its recurrence at the time of his action and its naming as He named—since He did not force him to his action nor compel him—makes good with it the command and prohibition and punishment and reward. And whoever denies this his creation clings to this type of illusion. And it is right that he consider the aspect by which the creation of things is known; if its verification is possible, then denial of this type is ignorance of wisdom. And upon that was the first thing he was created. So he will know if he submits to the One honored him, God willing, and if it is not possible, the question falls, and everything with which he confronted is set aside. And there is no power but through God.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the Maturidi position on human action and its compatibility with divine decree and command. The author begins by stating the foundational principle: the servant has a real action (fi‘l) in truth (fi al-haqiqa), and he has choice (mukhtar). The servant is the one who prefers (āthara) things and loves them. However, the creation (khalq) of that action did not compel him, nor force him—meaning God's creative power does not negate the servant's genuine choice. The passage then argues that given this reality, divine knowledge, record in the Preserved Tablet (al-lawh al-mahfuz), and the return of the action at its proper time, together with the fact that God did not force the servant, it is appropriate (hasan) for God to issue commands and prohibitions, and to reward and punish. Whoever denies this aspect of creation (i.e., that human acts are created by God in a way that still allows free choice) is holding an illusion. Such a person should consider how the creation of things is known; if they cannot verify their denial, then their denial is ignorance of wisdom (hikma). The passage concludes by noting that this is the original constitution of humanity (jubila 'alayh), and that if one submits, they will know, otherwise the objection falls away. The phrase 'There is no power but through God' (la quwwata illa billah) closes the argument, acknowledging divine assistance.

Misconception: Some hold that if God creates all actions, humans have no real choice and are forced, making divine command unjust.
Correction: Maturidi argues that God's creation of an action does not imply compulsion; the servant has genuine choice and preference, so commands and punishments are just.

Checkpoint

A) God forces all human actions
B) The servant has real action and choice, and God does not compel
C) Human actions are illusions
D) The Preserved Tablet contains only good deeds

Key Takeaway
Maturidi maintains that human beings have genuine choice and action, making divine commands and judgment just, even though God creates all actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Kaabi's Objections on Divine Creation
Al-Kaabi presents five objections against God creating evil acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objections raised by al-Kaabi against the Maturidi view that God creates all things, including evil acts.

Core Idea
Al-Kaabi argues that God's creation cannot include evil because that would contradict His praise and justice.

Before we can appreciate Maturidi's refutation, we need to hear the objections clearly. Al-Kaabi, a Mu'tazili theologian, raises several scriptural and rational arguments to exclude evil acts from God's creation. This lesson presents those objections as the starting point for the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Kaabi's first objection
Al-Kaabi argues that the verse 'Creator of everything' (Qur'an 6:102) is a praise (imtidaah), so it cannot include blameworthy acts like reviling God or killing prophets.
2
Second objection: God punishes disbelief
Al-Kaabi says God blames disbelief and punishes it; if He were its creator, that would be inconsistent with His justice.
3
Third and fourth objections
He claims the verse is specific to good things, and that the pronoun 'He' excludes human actions. He also notes that ugly deeds were not mentioned by the Prophet.
These objections rely on a restricted interpretation of 'everything'.
4
Fifth objection and the hadith
Al-Kaabi argues that the Qadariyya (those who deny divine creation of actions) are called the Magians of this ummah, implying his view is orthodox.
He cites the hadith to support his position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ذكر الكعبي أسئلتنا من ذلك قوله تعالى: خالق كل شيء، وأعمال العباد أشياء. فزعم أن ذا امتداح، وليس ذلك في شتم نفسه ولا في الكفر به ولا في قتل الأنبياء. والثاني أنه عاب الكفر وعذب عليه، ولا يجوز ذلك على ما يفعله. وقال: خصصنا أيضاً بما تلونا من الآيات. ودليل ذلك أنه لم يدخل هو في ذلك، وهو شيء؛ مع وجود آيات ذلك مخرجها، وهي خاصة. وبعد، فإن القبائح لم تذكر في هذا على عهد رسول الله، وإنما ذكر في الجواهر المورثة؛ وقال: بل قول المجوس أن الله أراد شيئًا مما هي محرمة في الإسلام، ولذلك قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «القدرية مجوس هذه الأمة». قال الشيخ رحمه الله: نقول وبالله التوفيق إذ ثبت أن الآية بحق الامتداح، كان في خروج شيء من الكائنات امتداحاً بغير الذي له، أو بما يشاركه فيه كل ضعيف؛ لأنه لو أراد كلية الأشياء ولم يكن خلقها، فامتدح بغير الذي له، وذلك كذب. وفي إخراج البعض مساواة غيره في أنه صانع كل شيء، يريد ما لا صنع له فيه، وذلك فاسد. مع ما لو جاز ذلك على الصرف إلى الغير الذي هو فعل للغير ليجوز أن يقال: ليس بخالق شيء، على أنه ليس بخالق ما هو فعل لغيره. فإذ كان وصفا له بالذم والعبودة ثبت أن الأول وصف له بالمدح والربوبية، وفي التخصيص إيجاب الأول. وأيضا إنه قال: هو رب كل شيء وإله كل شيء، وهو على كل شيء وكيل، ولم يجز إخراج شيء عن ذلك؛ وإن كان لا يليق القول به على التخصيص لقبح، نحو أن يقال: رب الخبائث وإله القبائح ووكيل الشياطين وإبليس، وقائم على كل نتن وقذر، فمثله الأول، وإن كان يقبح على التخصيص في أشياء من حيث التسمية. وبهذا الوجه الذي قال شهدت المجوس والزنادقة أن الله تعالى لم يخلق مؤذياً ولا فسادًا، ولا أمات وليا ولا أ?سى عدوا ولا أبقى الشياطين، ولا أعطى من يعلم أنه يشتمه ويصد عن طاعته أحدا قوة. ذكرنا ذلك ليعلموا أن أصل الاعتزال مقدر عن ذلك؛ إذ إليه فزعهم عند مخالفتهم المفهوم من القرآن ومما جرى عليه قول الإسلام، ولذلك قال رسول الله عليه السلام: «القدرية مجوس هذه الأمة».
Then al-Kaabi mentioned our questions: Among that is His saying: 'Creator of everything', and the deeds of servants are things. So he claimed that this is a praise, and that is not in reviling Himself nor in disbelief in Him nor in killing the prophets. And the second is that He blamed disbelief and punished it, and that is not permissible for what He does. And he said: We also specified by the verses we recited. And the proof of that is that 'He' is not included in that, and 'He' is a thing; while there are verses whose meaning is that they are specific. And after that, ugly deeds were not mentioned in this from the covenant of the Messenger of God, but mentioned in inherited precious jewels. And he said: Rather the saying of the Magians is that God willed something of what is forbidden in Islam, and therefore the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, said: 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this community.' The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: We say, and with God is success: When it is established that the verse is truly for praise, it would be in excluding something from existences a praise for other than what belongs to Him, or by what every weakling shares with Him. Because if He intended all things and did not create them, then He would praise for other than what belongs to Him, and that is a lie. And in excluding some, it equals others in that He is the maker of everything, but He intends what He has no making in, and that is corrupt. Moreover, if that were allowed for referring to another who is action for another, it would be permissible to say: He is not a creator of anything, on the basis that He is not the creator of what is the action of another. So since that would be a description of Him with blame and servitude, it is established that the first is a description of Him with praise and lordship, and in the specification it necessitates the first. And also He said: 'He is the Lord of everything and the God of everything, and He is over everything a guardian', and it is not permissible to exclude anything from that; even if it is not fitting to say it with specification due to ugliness, such as saying: Lord of filths and God of obscenities and Guardian of devils and Iblis, and Standing over every rot and filth, so the like of the first, even if it is ugly when specified for some things in terms of naming. And by this aspect that he said, the Magians and heretics testified that God did not create anything harmful nor corruption, nor caused a saint to die nor made an enemy [weak?], nor left the devils, nor gave power to anyone whom He knows will revile Him and turn away from obedience. We mentioned that so that they know that the origin of Mu'tazilism is derived from that; for they have recourse to it when they contradict what is understood from the Qur'an and what the saying of Islam has proceeded upon, and therefore the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, said: 'The Qadriyya are the Magians of this community.'
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer structure. The author first reports objections from al-Kaabi against the Maturidi view that God is the creator of all things, including evil acts. Al-Kaabi argues that the verse 'Creator of everything' (Qur'an 6:102) is only praise and does not include things like reviling God or killing prophets, because those would not be praiseworthy. He also argues that God blames and punishes disbelief, so He cannot be its creator. He claims that the verse is specifically for good things, not evil acts. He further argues that the pronoun 'He' in the verse refers only to God, not to human actions, and that ugly deeds were not discussed by the Prophet. He cites a hadith that Qadariyya are the Magians of this ummah. The author then responds: If the verse is for praise, then excluding some existences from creation would mean God is praised for what He did not create, which is false. If some things are excluded, that makes others equal to God in creating those things, which is corrupt. If we say acts of servants are not created by God, one could deny God's creation entirely. Such specification would make God blameworthy. Since the verse 'He is Lord of everything' (Qur'an 6:164) and similar verses include everything, we cannot exclude anything, even if it sounds ugly to specify particular objects. The Magians and heretics took this view to its conclusion, denying that God creates harmful things. The author concludes that Mu'tazilism originates from such divergences from the plain meaning of the Qur'an and the consensus of Islam, confirming the hadith about Qadariyya being Magians.

Key Terms
خالق كل شيء
امتداح
قدرية
مجوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God cannot create evil because He is praised and punishes evil.
Correction: Al-Kaabi's objection wrongly assumes that divine creation of evil entails divine approval or injustice. Maturidi responds by affirming God's absolute sovereignty while distinguishing creation from moral approval.

Checkpoint
According to al-Kaabi, why does the verse 'Creator of everything' not include evil acts?
A) Evil acts are not things
B) The verse is a praise, and evil acts are blameworthy
C) The Prophet forbade discussing evil acts
D) The Magians already used the same argument

Key Takeaway
Al-Kaabi's objections rest on the assumption that divine creation of evil contradicts God's praise and justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter suffering or evil in your life, do you ever find yourself subtly denying God's complete control over that event to protect your image of a 'good' God? How does Al-Kaabi's instinct to exclude evil from God's creation mirror a tendency in your own heart? Commit to reciting 'Creator of everything' while consciously including the difficult moments.
?
Al-Kaabi argued that God cannot create what He punishes. Have you ever assumed that your sins are somehow outside God's plan because you cannot bear the thought that He willed them? How does this lesson challenge your desire to limit God's sovereignty to preserve your own comfort? Write down one area you have been slow to submit to His complete will.
?
The hadith equates denying God's creation of actions with Magian dualism. Are there areas of your life where you functionally believe in two sources—God for good, and something else for evil? How can you repent from this subtle shirk and acknowledge that everything, including your trials, comes from Him? Resolve to thank Him for a specific difficulty today.

Divine Decree
intermediate
7 min
Maturidi's Refutation of Al-Kaabi
Maturidi refutes al-Kaabi's objections with logical arguments for absolute divine creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical refutation of al-Kaabi's objections, establishing that God is the creator of all things including evil acts.

Core Idea
If God is not the creator of all things, then praise for creation would be false and others would share in divinity.

Maturidi now responds to al-Kaabi's objections with a series of logical arguments. He shows that restricting God's creation leads to absurd consequences: either God is praised for what He did not do, or others become creators alongside Him. This lesson unpacks Maturidi's rigorous reasoning and its implications for divine unity.

Guided Path
1
First argument: praise requires creation
If the verse is praise, then excluding some things means God is praised for what He did not create, which is false.
2
Second argument: avoiding associationism
If some things are not created by God, then others are equal to Him in creating those things, which is corrupt (fasid).
3
Third argument: slippery slope
If we exclude human acts, one could deny God creates anything, leading to atheism.
4
Fourth argument: specification leads to blame
Specifying God as Lord of all but excluding ugly things would make Him blameworthy, which is impossible.
5
Conclusion: Magians and Mu'tazilism
Maturidi shows that al-Kaabi's view leads to the same conclusion as Magians, confirming the hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ذكر الكعبي أسئلتنا من ذلك قوله تعالى: خالق كل شيء، وأعمال العباد أشياء. فزعم أن ذا امتداح، وليس ذلك في شتم نفسه ولا في الكفر به ولا في قتل الأنبياء. والثاني أنه عاب الكفر وعذب عليه، ولا يجوز ذلك على ما يفعله. وقال: خصصنا أيضاً بما تلونا من الآيات. ودليل ذلك أنه لم يدخل هو في ذلك، وهو شيء؛ مع وجود آيات ذلك مخرجها، وهي خاصة. وبعد، فإن القبائح لم تذكر في هذا على عهد رسول الله، وإنما ذكر في الجواهر المورثة؛ وقال: بل قول المجوس أن الله أراد شيئًا مما هي محرمة في الإسلام، ولذلك قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «القدرية مجوس هذه الأمة». قال الشيخ رحمه الله: نقول وبالله التوفيق إذ ثبت أن الآية بحق الامتداح، كان في خروج شيء من الكائنات امتداحاً بغير الذي له، أو بما يشاركه فيه كل ضعيف؛ لأنه لو أراد كلية الأشياء ولم يكن خلقها، فامتدح بغير الذي له، وذلك كذب. وفي إخراج البعض مساواة غيره في أنه صانع كل شيء، يريد ما لا صنع له فيه، وذلك فاسد. مع ما لو جاز ذلك على الصرف إلى الغير الذي هو فعل للغير ليجوز أن يقال: ليس بخالق شيء، على أنه ليس بخالق ما هو فعل لغيره. فإذ كان وصفا له بالذم والعبودة ثبت أن الأول وصف له بالمدح والربوبية، وفي التخصيص إيجاب الأول. وأيضا إنه قال: هو رب كل شيء وإله كل شيء، وهو على كل شيء وكيل، ولم يجز إخراج شيء عن ذلك؛ وإن كان لا يليق القول به على التخصيص لقبح، نحو أن يقال: رب الخبائث وإله القبائح ووكيل الشياطين وإبليس، وقائم على كل نتن وقذر، فمثله الأول، وإن كان يقبح على التخصيص في أشياء من حيث التسمية. وبهذا الوجه الذي قال شهدت المجوس والزنادقة أن الله تعالى لم يخلق مؤذياً ولا فسادًا، ولا أمات وليا ولا أ?سى عدوا ولا أبقى الشياطين، ولا أعطى من يعلم أنه يشتمه ويصد عن طاعته أحدا قوة. ذكرنا ذلك ليعلموا أن أصل الاعتزال مقدر عن ذلك؛ إذ إليه فزعهم عند مخالفتهم المفهوم من القرآن ومما جرى عليه قول الإسلام، ولذلك قال رسول الله عليه السلام: «القدرية مجوس هذه الأمة».
Then al-Kaabi mentioned our questions: Among that is His saying: 'Creator of everything', and the deeds of servants are things. So he claimed that this is a praise, and that is not in reviling Himself nor in disbelief in Him nor in killing the prophets. And the second is that He blamed disbelief and punished it, and that is not permissible for what He does. And he said: We also specified by the verses we recited. And the proof of that is that 'He' is not included in that, and 'He' is a thing; while there are verses whose meaning is that they are specific. And after that, ugly deeds were not mentioned in this from the covenant of the Messenger of God, but mentioned in inherited precious jewels. And he said: Rather the saying of the Magians is that God willed something of what is forbidden in Islam, and therefore the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, said: 'The Qadariyya are the Magians of this community.' The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: We say, and with God is success: When it is established that the verse is truly for praise, it would be in excluding something from existences a praise for other than what belongs to Him, or by what every weakling shares with Him. Because if He intended all things and did not create them, then He would praise for other than what belongs to Him, and that is a lie. And in excluding some, it equals others in that He is the maker of everything, but He intends what He has no making in, and that is corrupt. Moreover, if that were allowed for referring to another who is action for another, it would be permissible to say: He is not a creator of anything, on the basis that He is not the creator of what is the action of another. So since that would be a description of Him with blame and servitude, it is established that the first is a description of Him with praise and lordship, and in the specification it necessitates the first. And also He said: 'He is the Lord of everything and the God of everything, and He is over everything a guardian', and it is not permissible to exclude anything from that; even if it is not fitting to say it with specification due to ugliness, such as saying: Lord of filths and God of obscenities and Guardian of devils and Iblis, and Standing over every rot and filth, so the like of the first, even if it is ugly when specified for some things in terms of naming. And by this aspect that he said, the Magians and heretics testified that God did not create anything harmful nor corruption, nor caused a saint to die nor made an enemy [weak?], nor left the devils, nor gave power to anyone whom He knows will revile Him and turn away from obedience. We mentioned that so that they know that the origin of Mu'tazilism is derived from that; for they have recourse to it when they contradict what is understood from the Qur'an and what the saying of Islam has proceeded upon, and therefore the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, said: 'The Qadriyya are the Magians of this community.'
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer structure. The author first reports objections from al-Kaabi against the Maturidi view that God is the creator of all things, including evil acts. Al-Kaabi argues that the verse 'Creator of everything' (Qur'an 6:102) is only praise and does not include things like reviling God or killing prophets, because those would not be praiseworthy. He also argues that God blames and punishes disbelief, so He cannot be its creator. He claims that the verse is specifically for good things, not evil acts. He further argues that the pronoun 'He' in the verse refers only to God, not to human actions, and that ugly deeds were not discussed by the Prophet. He cites a hadith that Qadariyya are the Magians of this ummah. The author then responds: If the verse is for praise, then excluding some existences from creation would mean God is praised for what He did not create, which is false. If some things are excluded, that makes others equal to God in creating those things, which is corrupt. If we say acts of servants are not created by God, one could deny God's creation entirely. Such specification would make God blameworthy. Since the verse 'He is Lord of everything' (Qur'an 6:164) and similar verses include everything, we cannot exclude anything, even if it sounds ugly to specify particular objects. The Magians and heretics took this view to its conclusion, denying that God creates harmful things. The author concludes that Mu'tazilism originates from such divergences from the plain meaning of the Qur'an and the consensus of Islam, confirming the hadith about Qadariyya being Magians.

Key Terms
خالق كل شيء
امتداح
مجوس
الاعتزال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's creation of evil means He approves of evil or is unjust.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes between creation and moral approval: God creates all actions, but judges them according to His wisdom and justice.

Checkpoint
What is the main logical flaw Maturidi identifies in al-Kaabi's objection?
A) Al-Kaabi misunderstands the Arabic grammar
B) Excluding some things from creation leads to shirk or false praise
C) The hadith is weak in authenticity
D) Al-Kaabi did not consider the attributes of God

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that absolute divine creation is necessary to preserve God's praise, lordship, and unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship or commit a sin, do you truly believe that God created that moment for a wise purpose, or do you subtly shift blame to others or yourself, denying His absolute creation? How will you repent and affirm His lordship over every detail of your life?
?
The Magians argued that God does not create harmful things. In what ways do you complain about your trials as if they were not from Him? Will you accept His decree over things you find ugly, trusting His wisdom that He is Lord of all? What practical step will you take to cultivate contentment with His creation of both good and difficult circumstances?
?
Maturidi warns that specifying God's creation only to good things leads to shirk. Examine your own heart: do you praise God only when things go well, and attribute failures to yourself or others? How does this passage challenge you to see His hand in every event, good and bad? What concrete change will you make in your attitude towards difficult situations starting today?

Exposing Inconsistency: The Analogy of Divine Presence
Maturidi refutes an objection using an analogy of spatial presence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's method of refuting an opponent's deflection by exposing inconsistency in their own principles.

Core Idea
Inconsistency in general statements reveals flawed theology.

Maturidi encounters an opponent who avoids the direct ruling of a verse by shifting to a discussion about types of actions. To expose the flaw, Maturidi draws an analogy: just as someone who says 'God is everywhere' would refuse to apply that statement to filthy places, the opponent's general affirmation of God's lordship collapses when applied to specific evil actions. This lesson unpacks that logical move.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English translation to grasp the structure of the objection and response.
Notice the shift from the verse's ruling to the type of actions.
2
Identify the analogy
The author uses the analogy of 'God is in every place' but not in dirty places. Recognize how this mirrors the opponent's position.
The analogy is central to the refutation.
3
Analyze the logical force
Consider why the inconsistency undermines the opponent's position and what principle is being defended.
Think about the principle of divine lordship and action attribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من حَيْده أن سأل عن حكم الآية، فأعرض عن ذلك واشتغل في الإجابة عن نوع الأفعال. وحقيقته أن يقول به في الجملة، وعند التفسير فيما يقبح لا يقول؛ كما يقول: الله في كل مكان؛ فإذا سئل عنه في الحُشُوش والأمكنة القذرة أبي ذلك. ثم هو رب كل شيء وإله كل شيء، ثم عند التفسير فيما يقبح يأبى، إلا أنه لما يقصر بحيث ذلك الإطلاق يدفع أصله، فمثله ما نحن فيه. والله الموفق.
Then from his deflection, that he asked about the ruling of the verse, but he turned away from that and engaged in answering about the type of actions. The truth of the matter is that he affirms it in general terms, but when it comes to interpretation regarding what is ugly/evil, he does not affirm it; just as he says: 'God is in every place'; but when asked about Him in latrines and filthy places, he refuses that. Then He is the Lord of everything and the God of everything, but when it comes to interpretation regarding what is ugly, he refuses, except that when one shortens [the expression] to the extent that that absolute statement repels its own principle, so similar is our case. And God is the Giver of success.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponent (likely the Qadariyya) deflects from the actual ruling of the verse and instead discusses the type of actions. The truth is that they affirm the verse in general but when it comes to specific ugly actions they deny it, analogous to saying 'God is everywhere' but refusing to say He is in dirty places. This inconsistency shows that their general affirmation repels their own principle. The argument is that their method is self-defeating.

Key Terms
الحكم
التفسير
الجملة
الإطلاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Absolute statements about God (like 'Lord of everything') can be qualified without consequence.
Correction: If a general statement is true, its implications must be accepted consistently; otherwise, the principle is self-contradictory.

Checkpoint

A) Saying God is everywhere but denying He is in dirty places
B) Saying God is one but affirming multiple gods
C) Saying God is merciful but believing in predestination
D) Saying God is all-powerful but denying miracles

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses an analogy of 'God is everywhere' to show that an opponent's general affirmation contradicts their specific denial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What specific situation comes to mind? How can you begin to apply the principle consistently, even when it challenges your comfort?
?
Imagine someone asks you about God's role in a recent tragedy. Do you, like the opponent Maturidi describes, deflect to general statements without allowing the specific application? What would it cost you to honestly attribute that tragedy to God's decree while still affirming His justice? Are you willing to bear that intellectual and spiritual tension?

Refuting the Qadariyya on 'Allah Created You and What You Do'
Maturidi refutes the Qadariyya interpretation of a key verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's argument that the apparent meaning of the verse refers to the creation of human actions, not idols, and his refutation of the Qadariyya's misinterpretation.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's apparent meaning cannot be diverted without clear evidence.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses a key argument from the Qadariyya, who claimed that the verse 'And Allah created you and what you do' (37:96) refers to idols, not human actions. He insists that the apparent meaning is the creation of action and cannot be reinterpreted without proof. He then exposes the logical inconsistency in their position and criticizes their use of another verse to deny the creation of actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's claim
Begin by reading the opponent's interpretation of the verse: that 'what you do' means idols. Notice how they link it to other verses about carving and fabricating.
The opponent is the Qadariyya who denied that Allah creates human actions.
2
Analyze Maturidi's first refutation
Maturidi argues that the apparent meaning is creation of action, and it cannot be diverted without evidence. He points out that the polytheists were rebuked for their actions (carving and worshipping), not merely for the objects.
This establishes that they worshipped their own actions, implying actions are created.
3
Follow the second refutation
Maturidi then criticizes their use of the Bahira verse (5:103) to deny creation of actions, showing that the verse refers to entities (camels), not actions.
This reveals their refusal to accept Allah's report.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how Maturidi upholds the apparent meaning and uses logical consistency to refute the misinterpretation.
This is a model of kalam argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج لخصمه بقوله: {والله خلقكم وما تعملون} [الصافات 96/37]، قال: يريد به آلهتم كقوله: {أتعبدون ما تنحتون} [الصافات 95/37]، وكقوله: {تلقف ما يأفكون}. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: نقول وبالله التوفيق: ظاهر الآية ذكر خلق العمل، فلم يجز صرف ذلك إلى غيره إلا بالبيان. مع ما في جميع ما ذكر نحتهم داخل، وكذلك إفكهم كما ذكر، وبه عُوبوا لا بذلك الشيء فقط، حيث فعلوا ثم عبدوا، فكأنهم عبدوا فعلهم، فمثله ما نحن فيه. وأيضا إنه لو صُرّح بالآية الهتهم بعد أن ذكر معمولاً، فإذا لم يكن الله خلق العمل لم يجز له القول بخلقه معمولاً، إذ ليس هو كذلك مخلوقاً؛ ثبت أن العمل مخلوق، ليعبدوا مخلوقا معمولاً كما ذكر. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم من عظم سفههم أن احتجوا بقوله تعالى: {ما جعل الله من بحيرة} الآية، وتلك أسماء تلك الأعيان في نفي خلق الأعمال، بقوله: {ما جعل كذا}. وهن أسماء تلك الأعيان لا الأفعال، وهن مخلوقات لا شك. ثم يرد فيما ذكر العمل بالخلق إلى حقيقة الأعيان ليدفع خلق الأفعال. فهذا يبيّن أن رأيهم أن لا يقبلوا عن الله خبره، ولا يرجعوا في أمر إلى تدبيره. ذلك. والله أسأل العصمة.
Then he argued on behalf of his opponent with His saying: {And Allah created you and what you do} [Saffat 37:96], he said: He means by it your idols, like His saying: {Do you worship what you carve} [Saffat 37:95], and like His saying: {It swallows what they fabricate}. The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: We say, and with Allah is success: The apparent meaning of the verse is the mention of the creation of action, so it is not permissible to divert that to something else without evidence. Moreover, all that he mentioned of their carving is included, and likewise their fabrication as mentioned, and they were rebuked for that, not merely for that thing; for they did [it] and then worshipped, so it is as if they worshipped their own action, so similar is our case. And also, if the verse explicitly referred to their idols after mentioning a direct object, then if Allah did not create the action, it would not be permissible for him to say that He created it as an object, since it is not created in that way; it is established that the action is created, so that they worship a created object as mentioned. And there is no power except with Allah. Then among the greatness of their foolishness is that they argued with His saying: {Allah has not made any Bahira} [Ma'idah 5:103] — and those are names of those entities — to negate the creation of actions, by saying: 'He has not made such and such.' And they are names of those entities, not actions, and they are undoubtedly created. Then he redirects the mention of action with creation to the reality of entities to repulse the creation of actions. This shows that their opinion is that they do not accept Allah's report, nor do they refer in any matter to His planning. That is so. And I ask Allah for protection.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from the opponent (the Qadariyya) who interprets the verse 'Allah created you and what you do' as referring to idols, not human actions. Imam Maturidi refutes this: the apparent meaning of the verse is the creation of action, and it cannot be diverted without clear evidence. He points out that the polytheists' carving and worshipping were criticized; they worshipped their own actions. If the action were not created, they would be worshipping something uncreated, but it is established that the action is created. Then he criticizes their use of the 'Bahira' verse to deny creation of actions, arguing that the verse speaks about entities (camels) not actions. This shows their refusal to accept Allah's report.

ظاهر الآية
الآلهة
نحتم
إفك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qadariyya believe that 'what you do' in the verse refers to idols, not human actions, and that actions are not created by Allah.
Correction: Imam Maturidi shows that the apparent meaning is creation of action, and the verse cannot be reinterpreted without clear evidence. The polytheists' sin involved their own actions, implying actions are created.

B) Allah created you and your actions
C) Allah created you and your wealth
D) Allah created you and your children

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi defends the apparent meaning of the Qur'an and argues that human actions are created by Allah, refuting the Qadariyya misinterpretation.

Maturidi says that the Qadariyya's opinion shows they do not accept Allah's report. Reflect: In which areas of your life do you truly accept Allah's words, and in which areas do you resist? What would it take for you to completely surrender to what Allah has revealed?

Refutation of the Objection about Shared Creation
Maturidi responds to the objection that if actions are created, they resemble God's creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that human actions do not resemble Allah's creation despite both involving origination.

Core Idea
Similarity in origination does not imply resemblance due to differing aspect and meaning.

Maturidi responds to a key objection: if human actions are created, they would look like Allah's creation. He emphasizes that human actions are inherently different—vain and corrupt—while Allah's actions are wise and good. The mere fact of occurring does not create resemblance, just as 'knowing' differs between God and humans.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objection from the Qur'anic verse and Maturidi's immediate response.
Pay attention to the structure: objection, then multiple layers of answer.
2
Identify the argument layers
Find the three main points Maturidi makes: (1) actions differ in nature, (2) occurrence alone doesn't cause resemblance, (3) even if there's shared meaning, resemblance only in entities.
The third point is nuanced—resemblance in attributes that subsist in entities is possible but not applicable here.
3
Understand the irony
Notice the final sentence: the opponents charge Maturidi with tashbih, but they themselves equate human and divine actions in reality. This is a rhetorical counter.
This highlights the inconsistency in the opponents' position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: واحتجوا أيضا بقوله: {أم جعلوا الله شركاء خلقوا كخلقه} [الرعد 13/16]، الآية، إنه على قولكم يشبه فعلكم خلقه. فقال: معاذ الله، بل فعلنا عبث وفساد وخضوع وذلة، وفعله حكمة وصواب وتفضل وتطوّل. قال: وليس من حيث الحدث والحدث والخروج من العدم تشابه لاختلاف الجهة، كما لم يكن في عالم وعالم وحي وقادر لاختلاف المعنى. قال: وبعد، فإن فعلنا يخالف فعل الله لعينه. ثم في الإيجاد والإحداث معنى يوجب التشابه، وإنما يجوز ذلك في الأعيان بما يحل فيها. مع ما يعارض بقول جهم حيث قال: في تحقيق الفعل تشابه. ثم قال: العجب من إلزامهم التشبيه بالإحداث، ولم يلزموا أنفسهم فيما فعلوا فعل ربهم في الحقيقة.
He said: And they also argued with His saying: {Or have they set up partners with Allah who create as He creates} [Ra'd 13:16], the verse, that according to your statement, your actions resemble His creation. He said: God forbid! Rather, our actions are vanity, corruption, submission, and humiliation, while His action is wisdom, correctness, favor, and bounty. He said: And there is not, from the perspective of occurrence and occurrence and coming out of non-existence, any resemblance due to the difference of aspect, just as there is not in knowing and knowing, and living and powerful, due to the difference of meaning. He said: And moreover, our action differs from Allah's action in its very essence. Then, in bringing into existence and originating there is a meaning that necessitates resemblance, but that is only permissible in entities due to what subsists in them. Along with what opposes the statement of Jahm, where he said: In the realization of action there is resemblance. Then he said: The wonder is that they impose the charge of resemblance (tashbih) due to origination, and they do not impose upon themselves that they do the action of their Lord in actuality.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponents argue that if human actions are created, they would resemble Allah's creation. The response: human actions are different in nature—they are vanity and corruption, while Allah's actions are wisdom. Moreover, the mere fact of occurring or coming from non-existence does not imply resemblance because the aspect (جهة) differs. Just as 'knowing' can describe two beings but with different meanings. The action's essence is different. Even if there is a shared meaning of bringing into existence, resemblance is only in attributes that subsist in entities. The author then notes that the Jahmiyya claim resemblance in action, and it is ironic that the opponents charge others with tashbih while they themselves equate human and divine actions in reality.

Key Terms
تشابه
الإحداث
الحكمة
العبث
الجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that similarity in the concept of 'origination' implies literal resemblance between God and creation.
Correction: Maturidi explains that the aspect and meaning differ, preventing any real resemblance. Even if there is a shared concept, it does not entail similarity in essence.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why do human actions not resemble Allah's actions even though both involve origination?
A) Because human actions are voluntary and Allah's are involuntary
B) Because the aspect (جهة) and meaning differ
C) Because human actions are not actually created
D) Because Allah's actions are eternal

Key Takeaway
The mere fact that both human and divine actions involve origination does not make them resemble each other; the aspect and meaning differ fundamentally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your day or make decisions, do you ever unconsciously assume that your act of choosing is comparable to Allah's will? How does Maturidi's distinction between aspect and essence challenge that subtle arrogance?
?
You have likely heard someone say 'I am the lord of my own life' or 'I create my own destiny.' How does this lesson expose the hidden tashbih in such statements? What small step can you take today to acknowledge the difference between your actions and His?
?
Think of a time you criticized another person's actions harshly. Did you treat their mistake as if it were an offense against absolute wisdom, as if you were judging from a divine perspective? How can Maturidi's contrast between human actions (عبث وفساد) and divine actions (حكمة وصواب) humble you in that moment?

The Kasb Objection: Distinguishing the Acquired Act
Maturidi responds to an objection about distinguishing the acquired act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's initial response to the objection that the acquired act cannot be distinguished from other acts, and why indistinguishability does not negate divine creation.

Core Idea
Indistinguishability does not disprove divine creation.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses a new objection against the concept of kasb (acquisition). The opponent argues that if the acquired act cannot be distinguished from other acts, then how can it be a separate category? Maturidi begins his answer by pointing out that even if they are indistinguishable, both acts are still created by God. However, the passage cuts off before the full answer is given.

Guided Path

3

Maturidi says if the acts are confused so reality is unknown, it would be established that both are created together. What does this imply?
The answer is incomplete; consider what might follow.

ثم عارض : هل يُعرف به المكتسب من غيره ؟ قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وهي المعارضة إن اشتبه المكتسب لغيره حتى لا يُعرف حقيقة واحد منهما [ولكان قد] ثبت أنهما مخلوقان معا لذلك. وبعد، فإنه ليس على معرفة

The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: And it is an objection: if the acquired act is confused with other than it so that the reality of neither is known, [and it would have been] established that both are created together for that reason. Commentary
This passage begins a new objection about the acquired act (muktasab) in human action. The opponent questions whether the acquired act can be distinguished from other acts. Maturidi's answer begins by stating that if the acquired act is confused with other acts so that the reality of neither is known, then it would be established that both are created together. Unfortunately, the text cuts off at the end of the page: 'afterwards, he is not upon knowledge' is incomplete. The argument likely continues to assert that indistinguishability does not affect the fact that both are created by God.
