Issue: The statement on the suitability of power for opposites and the imposition of what cannot be borne (taklif ma la yutaq). The jurist Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Then the people of this view differed concerning the power for obedience: is it suitable for disobedience or not? A group said: It is suitable for both matters, and that is the saying of Abu Hanifa and a group. And this saying is affirmed by all the Mu'tazila upon reflection, and the saying of imposing what cannot be borne is verified against them, and that is the reason for their saying that power precedes. And God is the grantor of success. And the basis of this is: since every cause of an act is suitable for the thing and its opposite, so too power—with what is in the negation that it is suitable for both matters, [i.e.] the loss of power over the opposite of that which one has come with, and one may be commanded and prohibited from it at its time—so it necessitates the saying that one has power over the thing and its opposite, so that the command and prohibition are within capacity and power. And there is no power except with God. Then the principle is: everything that is suitable for a thing and not suitable for its opposite is that by which [the act] occurs by nature not by choice. And if power were not suitable for both, then what occurs would occur by nature not by choice. And there is no power except with God. And a group among them said: The power for obedience is other than the power for disobedience, among them al-Husayn and others. And they hold that the power for obedience is tawfiq (divine facilitation) and `isma (preservation), and the power for disobedience is khidhlan (abandonment) and tark (letting go) according to what is chosen. And the evidence for that is the existence of the request for aid (ma`una) and preservation (isma) with the comprehension that there is no deviation with them, and tawfiq with the comprehension that with it is correctness. And likewise the apparent saying: 'O God, strengthen me for Your obedience and help me upon it,' and seeking refuge from abandonment and deviation. It is established that if each of them could produce what the other produces, then the one who asks [for guidance] would not be more entitled than the one who seeks refuge [from deviation]. And if preservation could have deviation, it would not be...
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal discussion on the nature of human power (qudra) in relation to obedience and disobedience. The author presents two positions. The first, attributed to Abu Hanifa and many others, holds that power is suitable for both opposite actions (obedience and disobedience). This is necessary to account for both command and prohibition: the same power can do either. If power only served one, the opposite act would be impossible, making the prohibition or command meaningless (taklif ma la yutaq). The Mu'tazila also held this view, but the author notes that it forces them to accept the notion of taklif ma la yutaq (obligation of what cannot be borne), which is problematic. The second view, from al-Husayn al-Najjar and others, distinguishes between power for obedience (which is divine facilitation and preservation) and power for disobedience (which is abandonment). They argue from the evidence of prayer: people ask God for strength and preservation in obedience and seek refuge from abandonment. If the same power could produce both, the prayer would be meaningless because the same power could be used for disobedience. Therefore, the two powers are distinct. The passage ends with a logical argument: if preservation (isma) could include deviation, then it would not truly be preservation.

Key Terms
التوفيق
العصمة
الخذلان
الترك
المعونة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all human actions come from the same power, so prayers for guidance are unnecessary.
Correction: The second view shows that obedience requires special divine grace, and prayers for aid are meaningful because the power for obedience is distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the second view (al-Husayn), what is the power for obedience?
A) Mere human effort.
B) Divine facilitation and preservation.
C) The same as power for disobedience.
D) Free will alone.

Key Takeaway
Obedience and disobedience stem from different kinds of divine support: tawfiq for obedience, khidhlan for disobedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you first recognize that you failed to seek God's help, or do you assume your own strength was enough? This lesson shows that obedience requires tawfiq. Are you truly asking God daily for strength for obedience, as the Companions did?
?
If God could leave you to your own choice (khidhlan) as a consequence of neglecting to seek His aid, how urgently should you repent and renew your reliance on Him? What specific supplication will you add to your daily prayers after this lesson?
?
Consider a recent act of disobedience. Could it be that God abandoned you to your own choice because you relied on yourself rather than Him? What will you change tonight to ensure you seek His tawfiq before every action?

The Unity of Tawfiq and 'Isma
Maturidi argues for the unity of tawfiq and 'isma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi proves that tawfiq (divine enabling) and 'isma (preservation) are essentially one and the same divine assistance for faith.

Core Idea
Tawfiq and 'isma are one: they are assistance to faith.

This lesson focuses on a key argument from Imam al-Maturidi that two central concepts in Islamic theology—tawfiq and 'isma—are not separate gifts but are in reality one and the same. Maturidi uses a simple observation: people ask for them just as they ask for assistance and strengthening, and no one attributes these qualities to a disbeliever. This points to their essential unity as assistance for faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two separate arguments Maturidi presents to prove the unity of tawfiq and 'isma.
The passage begins with a note about the heart's tranquility, which sets the stage for the first conclusion about distinct powers.
2
Identify the first argument: asking analogy
Maturidi observes that people ask for 'isma and tawfiq just as they ask for assistance and strengthening. This similarity implies that the pair are actually one in reality.
Ask yourself: why would two distinct gifts be asked for using the same pattern?
3
Identify the second argument: believer/disbeliever distinction
No one says a disbeliever is muwaffaq (successful) or ma'sum (protected) for belief; similarly, no one denies this for a believer. This shows that tawfiq and 'isma are essentially assistance to faith, and their opposite is khidhlan (abandonment).
This argument relies on common linguistic usage and consensus.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Together, these two arguments establish that tawfiq and 'isma are one reality: divine assistance to stay on the path of faith.
Reflect on why this unity matters for understanding divine action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا تبقى يطمئن القلب عند الوجود . فثبت أن قوة كل نوع من ذلك غير قوة النوع الآخر، وثبت بما يسأل العصمة والتوفيق كما يسأل المعونة والتقوية أنهما في الحقيقة واحد. وأيضا إنه لا أحد يطلق القول في الكافر أنه موفق للإيمان معصوم عن الكفر، ولا أحد يمتنع عنه في المؤمن؛ ثبت أن معنى ذلك المعونة على الإيمان، والآخر الخذلان وأيضاً إن القوة إذ هي وقتين لا يصلح بها الفعلان ولا سبيل إلى جمع الفعلين المتضادين في وقت واحد؛ ثبت أن ذلك قوة لأحدهما لا لهما، وأن الذي يكون لهما يبقى لاحتمالهما. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضاً إن القوة لا يجوز وجودها إلا وثمة اضطرار ، كالنار في التحريق والثلج في التبريد، إنه يقع به الذي له طبع بالاضطرار؛ وذلك كالولاية مع الإيمان، والعداوة مع الكفران، سببهما مختلف على اختلافهما، فمثله أمر القوة على الأمرين. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
It does not remain; the heart is assured at existence. So it is established that the power of each type of that is different from the power of the other type, and it is established by what is asked of al-ʿiṣma (preservation) and al-tawfīq (divine enabling) just as one asks for al-muʿāwana (assistance) and al-taqwiya (strengthening) that they are in reality one. And also that no one applies the statement to the disbeliever that he is muwaffaq (divinely enabled) for belief and maʿṣūm (preserved) from disbelief, nor does anyone refrain from it regarding the believer; it is established that the meaning of that is assistance to faith, and the other is khidhlān (abandonment). And also that when power exists in two moments, the two acts are not accomplished by it, and there is no way to combine two opposing acts in one moment; it is established that it is a power for one of them, not for both, and that which is for both would remain for its possibility for both. And there is no power except by Allah. And also that power cannot exist except with necessity (iḍṭirār), like fire in burning and snow in cooling, it occurs by that which has a nature necessarily; and that is like al-walāya (loyalty) with faith, and al-ʿadāwa (enmity) with disbelief, their causes are different according to their difference, so the matter of power over the two matters is similar. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is a proof that power (quwwa) for different types of acts is distinct, and that divine gifts like tawfiq (enabling) and 'isma (preservation) are essentially one. Maturidi begins by stating that the heart's tranquility at existence establishes that power for each type of act is different. He argues that when people ask for 'isma and tawfiq just as they ask for mu'awana (assistance) and taqwiya (strengthening), it shows they are one in reality. He supports this by noting that no one says a disbeliever has tawfiq for belief or 'isma from disbelief, and similarly for a believer—thus tawfiq is assistance to faith, and its opposite is khidhlan (abandonment). Then he argues that if power existed in two moments, it could not produce two opposing acts simultaneously, so it must be power for one act, not both. Finally, he likens power to natural forces like fire and snow that necessitate their effects by nature, concluding that the power for obedience and disobedience is distinct. The repeated phrase 'wa lā quwwata illā billāh' (and there is no power except by Allah) reinforces that all power is from Allah.

Correction: Maturidi proves they are in reality one: both are assistance to faith, and their opposite is divine abandonment.

A) That these are distinct gifts
B) That they are in reality one
C) That they are unnecessary for believers
D) That they only apply to disbelievers

Key Takeaway
Tawfiq and 'isma are essentially one; they are divine assistance for faith, and their opposite is khidhlan.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought that your faith is due to your own strength or choices? This lesson teaches that even preservation from disbelief (''isma) is purely divine assistance. Does pride in your faith linger in your heart? How will you cultivate gratitude to Allah today for keeping you in the fold of iman?
?
If tawfiq and ''isma are one and entirely from Allah, then your faith depends completely on His continuous support. Are you living as if this is true? Or does your life reflect an attitude that you are the one holding onto faith? What specific practice can you adopt to remind yourself of your absolute dependence on Him?
?
The opposite of this divine assistance is khidhlan (abandonment). Are there any actions or habits in your life that risk invoking khidhlan? What would it mean for you to be left to your own devices? Ask Allah to protect you from this and to grant you constant tawfiq.

Distinct Powers for Opposing Acts
Maturidi proves the distinctness of powers for opposing acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the power (quwwa) for each act is distinct and cannot be the same power for two opposing acts.

Core Idea
One power cannot produce two opposing acts; each act has its own power.

This lesson continues Maturidi's argument on divine power. He presents two proofs that the power for obedience and the power for disobedience must be distinct. The first proof is temporal: a single power existing at two different moments cannot accomplish two opposite acts simultaneously. The second proof is analogical: power operates by necessity, like fire burning or snow cooling, and since loyalty and enmity are opposite, their powers must be different. The lesson emphasizes that all power is from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two distinct proofs Maturidi offers for the distinctness of powers.
Pay attention to the repeated phrase 'wa la quwwata illa billah' which reinforces the source of all power.
2
Understand the temporal argument
Maturidi argues that if power existed in two moments, it could not produce two different acts in one moment; opposing acts cannot be combined. Therefore, the power must be for one act, not both.
Think about why this argument rules out a single power for both obedience and disobedience.
3
Understand the necessity argument
Power, like natural forces, necessitates its effect. Fire burns by nature, snow cools. Similarly, loyalty (walaya) accompanies faith and enmity (adawa) accompanies disbelief. Since their causes are different, the powers are different.
This argument uses the idea that power necessarily brings about its proper act.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Both arguments show that the power for faith and the power for disbelief are distinct. All power ultimately comes from Allah.
Consider the implications for human responsibility and divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Maturidi begins by stating that the heart's tranquility at existence establishes that power for each type of act is different. Refutation of Mu'tazilite View on Qudra and Implied Coercion
Maturidi critiques the Mu'tazilite position that qudra does not last, showing it implies compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazilite view on qudra reduces actions to compulsion and undermines moral responsibility.

Core Idea
If power is not present at action, then action is compelled, not free.

Maturidi begins by acknowledging that the Mu'tazilite position sounds plausible, but upon analysis it leads to logical absurdities. He argues that denying the persistence of qudra at the moment of action effectively makes the agent powerless and compelled, which contradicts the basis for moral categories like friendship and enmity with Allah. This passage shows how kalam refutes flawed premises by tracing their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that Maturidi starts by conceding the Mu'tazilite view sounds pleasant before refuting it.
The passage is a dense logical argument; take your time.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
Find where Maturidi states the Mu'tazilite position: that qudra does not last for two moments and is not present at the time of the act.
This is the premise that leads to compulsion.
3
Follow the deduction to compulsion
Maturidi argues that if power is absent at the time of action, the act occurs by necessity (idtirar) and nature (tab'), so the agent is compelled.
He compares this to losing all causes of an act.
4
Understand the absurd consequence
The Mu'tazilite view forces a situation where one becomes a friend or enemy of Allah under compulsion, which is morally and rationally monstrous.
This shows the theological stakes of the debate.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Ask yourself how Maturidi uses their own claim about inevitable movement to reinforce his point. This is a common kalam technique: reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر طرفًا مما يبين قبح قول المعتزلة عند التحصيل، وإن كان قولهم في الإطلاق قولاً لذيذا في السمع يشبه أن يكون حقا . والله الموفق. وهو أن من قولهم : إن القدرة لا تبقى وقتين وإنها ليست وقت الفعل. فوقع الفعل في الحقيقة ولا قوة له وقت وجوده، وذلك علم الاضطرار ووقوع الفعل بالطبع. ثم الدلالة أن حق مثله الاضطرار [و] أن فقد جميع الأسباب التي بها الفعل لوقته يحيل الفعل ويوصف صاحبه بالاضطرار فوت القدرة التي بها الفعل أحق بذلك ؛ فصيروه مضطرًا إلى ما يصير به وليا الله تعالى، وعدوا له عند الاختيار. ومما يوضح ذلك أيضاً أن من قولهم: إن من أراد التحرك للوقت الثاني منه أنها تقع لا محالة، ولا يقدر صرفها إلا بمنع من قبل غيره. وذلك آية الضرورة. ثم وجبت الولاية والعداوة بمثله، وذلك وحش في العقل.
Then we mention a portion that shows the ugliness of the Mu'tazilites' statement upon verification, although their statement in its absolute sense is a pleasant statement to the ear that seems to be true. Allah is the Granter of success. It is that among their statements: that qudra (power) does not last for two moments and that it is not at the time of the act. So the act occurs in reality while there is no power for it at the time of its existence, and that is the sign of necessity (iḍṭirār) and the occurrence of the act by nature. Then the indication is that the right of its like is necessity, and that the loss of all the causes by which the act exists at its time renders the act impossible, and its possessor is described as compelled, the loss of the power by which the act is more deserving of that; so they made him compelled to that by which he becomes a friend (walī) of Allah, the Exalted, and an enemy to Him at the time of choice. And among that which clarifies this also is that among their statements: that whoever intends movement for the second moment from him, it occurs inevitably, and he cannot turn it away except by prevention from another. That is the sign of necessity. Then loyalty (walāya) and enmity (ʿadāwa) are necessitated by its like, and that is monstrous in the intellect.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position that power (qudra) does not persist for two moments and that power is not present at the time of the act. Maturidi begins by noting that the Mu'tazilite statement sounds pleasant but is false upon verification. Their view is that power is not present when the act occurs, so the act happens without power—which is the mark of compulsion (iḍṭirār) and natural necessity. Maturidi argues that losing the causes that enable an act renders the act impossible and the agent compelled. Since the Mu'tazilites say power is lost at the time of the act, they effectively make the agent compelled in becoming a friend or enemy of Allah at the moment of choice. He further clarifies by citing their own claim that if someone intends to move at the next moment, it inevitably occurs unless prevented. This is a sign of necessity, and such a basis for loyalty or enmity is intellectually monstrous (وحش في العقل).

Key Terms

اضطرار
طبع
ولاية
عداوة
اختيار

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That power (qudra) does not need to be present at the time of action for the act to be voluntary and morally responsible.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if power is absent, the act occurs by necessity (idtirar) and nature, meaning the agent is compelled and not morally responsible.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say is the logical consequence of the Mu'tazilite view that qudra is not present at the time of action?
A) The act occurs freely
B) The agent is compelled (mudtar)
C) The act becomes impossible
D) The agent gains more power

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite view by showing that denying the persistence of qudra leads logically to compulsion, making moral categories like friendship and enmity to Allah meaningless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Maturidi warns that the Mu'tazilite view, though pleasant-sounding, leads to a monstrous conclusion. What pleasant-sounding belief about your own freedom or responsibility have you been comfortably holding, even though deeper reflection might reveal it removes your accountability before Allah? Will you honestly examine it today?
?
If you truly believed that your power to obey or disobey Allah vanished the moment before you acted, would you feel genuinely responsible for your deeds? How would that change your sense of urgency in seeking forgiveness and striving to change? Reflect on a specific sin you have struggled with—does this lesson make you realize you cannot blame a lack of power for your failure?
?
Maturidi shows that denying lasting qudra makes you compelled in the very acts that determine your friendship or enmity with Allah. In what area of your life have you been acting as if you are compelled—by habit, circumstance, or desire—rather than exercising the qudra Allah has given you? What is one concrete step you will take today to reclaim your agency and consciously choose loyalty to Allah?

Maturidi's Refutation of Mu'tazilite Command and Prohibition
Maturidi refutes Mu'tazilite claim that command and prohibition do not apply at the time of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the Mu'tazilite view on command and prohibition at the time of action leads to the invalidation of moral accountability and constitutes an impossible burden.

Core Idea
Command and prohibition must apply at the time of action or moral accountability collapses.

Imam Maturidi engages with a sophisticated Mu'tazilite claim that divine command and prohibition are only metaphorical at the moment of action. He shows that this view logically destroys the very basis of obedience and disobedience, and ultimately amounts to imposing an impossible burden. In this passage, he dissects the Mu'tazilite position and exposes its rational contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the full translation. Note the dense argumentative style.
The passage contains a refutation—track the flow of reasoning.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
The Mu'tazilites say there is no command or prohibition at the time of the act except metaphorically. Find the sentence that states this.
Look for the phrase 'ليس عليه وقت الفعل أمر ولا نهي إلا على المجاز'.
3
Trace Maturidi's rational principle
Maturidi invokes a rational principle: a command for a future time does not apply now. See how he applies this to the Mu'tazilite infinite regress.
4
Understand the conclusion
The Mu'tazilite view invalidates command and prohibition, and because they deny power at the time of act, it becomes taklif ma la yutaq.
This is the key theological critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن من قولهم : أن ليس عليه وقت الفعل أمر ولا نهي إلا على المجاز كما يقوله المسلمون . وإنما معناه أنه مأمور به بمعنى أن كان من قبل ليفعل فيه . فإذا لم يكن هو مأمورا به ولا منهيا لم يكن بالفعل مؤتمرا ولا مرتكبًا النهي ،وقته وبه تجب العداوة والولاية، فصارا في الحقيقة لا لطاعة ولا لمعصية، أو لا أمر ولا نهى ولا قوة إلا بالله . وعلى ذلك قولهم: إنه مأمور بالفعل في الوقت الثاني، وفي الثاني مأمور به في الوقت الثالث، كذلك أبدا. فلا يفعل الذي أمر بكل وقت وليس بتارك للأمر، لما ليس بمأمور وقت الترك. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم الأصل الذي في العقل دركه أن كل مأمور بالفعل للغَدِ ليس بمأمور هو به للحال، فكذلك [ا] يجب في الوقت القريب؛ فيجب أن الذي أمر بالفعل للوقت الذي يتلوه ليس بمأمور به للحال في العقل . ثم ليس بمأمور به في الوقت الثاني عندهم، ولا منهى عن ضده، فيبطل حقيقة الأمر والنهي بما / في العقل احتماله على قولهم، ويبطل قولهم بما في العقل دفعه . [١٣٩] وهم مع ذلك لا يجعلون له قدرة في ذلك الفعل، فيكون تكليف ما لا يطاق على قولهم. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And also among their statements: that there is no command or prohibition upon him at the time of the act except metaphorically, as the Muslims say. Rather, its meaning is that he is commanded in the sense that he was previously to act in it. So if he is not commanded or prohibited, he is not at the time of the act obedient nor committing the prohibition, at which time loyalty and enmity are necessitated, so they become in reality not for obedience nor for disobedience, or there is no command or prohibition, and there is no power except by Allah. And upon that is their statement: he is commanded for the act in the second moment, and in the second he is commanded for the third moment, and so on forever. So he does not do what he is commanded at each moment, and he is not abandoning the command, because he is not commanded at the time of abandonment. And there is no power except by Allah. Then the principle which the intellect grasps is that everyone commanded for an act tomorrow is not commanded for it now, likewise it is necessary in the near time; so it is necessary that the one who is commanded for an act for the time that follows is not commanded for it at present in the intellect. Then he is not commanded for it in the second moment according to them, nor prohibited from its opposite, so the reality of command and prohibition is invalidated by that which the intellect can conceive according to their statement, and their statement is invalidated by that which the intellect repels. And they also do not ascribe power to him in that act, so it becomes taklīf mā lā yutāq (imposition of what cannot be borne) according to their statement. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite view that command and prohibition do not apply at the exact time of the act except metaphorically. Maturidi explains that according to the Mu'tazila, the command is understood as having been given earlier, not at the moment of action. Consequently, at the time of the act, the agent is neither obedient nor disobedient, so loyalty or enmity based on the act becomes meaningless. Furthermore, the Mu'tazilites hold that a person is commanded for the next moment, and in that next moment commanded for the following moment, ad infinitum—so they never actually perform the commanded act at the commanded time, nor do they abandon it because they are not commanded at the abandonment time. Maturidi then states the rational principle: a person commanded for tomorrow is not commanded today; similarly, if commanded for the next moment, they are not commanded at the present moment. Since they are not commanded at the second moment according to the Mu'tazilites, nor prohibited from its opposite, the entire reality of command and prohibition collapses. Moreover, since the Mu'tazilites deny power at the time of the act, this amounts to imposing an impossible burden (taklif ma la yutaq).

Key Terms
أمر
نهي
مجاز
طاعة
معصية
تكليف ما لا يطاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi denies any metaphorical use of command; actually he argues that such a view destroys moral accountability.
Correction: Maturidi does not deny metaphorical command per se, but shows that if command is only metaphorical at the time of action, then obedience and disobedience become meaningless.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the Mu'tazilite view on command and prohibition at the time of action lead to?
A) Obedience and disobedience become clearly defined
B) The person is always obedient
C) The reality of command and prohibition is invalidated
D) The person is always disobedient

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the Mu'tazilite view collapses the moral structure of command and prohibition, making it impossible to assign obedience or disobedience and ultimately imposing an impossible burden.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you delay a command of Allah, thinking 'I will do it tomorrow,' are you not effectively treating the command as if it does not apply now? How does Maturidi's argument expose the flaw in such procrastination, and what will you do today to obey a command you have been postponing?
?
Consider a moment when you rationalized a sin by telling yourself 'the prohibition is not active for me right now' or 'I will repent later.' In light of this lesson, how can you ensure that you treat every moment as carrying its own command and prohibition? What specific step will you take this week to break that rationalization?
?
If you truly believed that obedience and disobedience are defined only by an active command at the very moment of action, would you approach your daily choices differently? What is one area of your life where you need to stop living as if the command is only for a future time, and start obeying now?

Maturidi's Principle on Act and Power
Maturidi dismisses a trivial debate and states his principle on act and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's principle that an act performed without power is not truly the agent's act, and how this relates to obligation and incapacity.

Core Idea
An act without power is not truly the agent's act.

Imam Maturidi dismisses a debate between the Mu'tazilites and al-Husayn al-Najjar about whether to call a capacity 'power', calling it meaningless. Instead, he provides a substantive principle: the existence of an act without power for the agent invalidates the act's meaning and transfers it to something else. This lesson unpacks that principle and its implications for obligation and incapacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's argument flow come through before you interpret.
Notice Maturidi's concise style.
2
Identify the dispute being dismissed
The passage starts by saying the dispute with al-Husayn has no meaning. Understand what that dispute is about: whether a disbeliever's capacity for obedience is called 'power' or not.
Maturidi thinks this is just a naming issue.
3
Understand the core principle
Maturidi states: 'the existence of the act while there is no power for the one who has the act upon him invalidates the meaning of the act and transfers it to something else.' This is the key point. Also, an act from an ignorant person is impermissible.
This principle links act, power, and agency.
4
Examine the parallel with obligation and incapacity
Maturidi draws a parallel: just as obligation (khitab) is based on knowledge, power is required for responsibility. The incapable (ajiz) is not obligated because he lacks the means of capacity, just as the insane are not obligated due to lacking knowledge.
This reinforces that moral responsibility requires both knowledge and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المسألة بينهم وبين الحسين لا معنی لها، لأن الحسين يقول : كل شيء يكون به فعل الطاعة كان مع الكافر سوى العصمة والتوفيق. وهم وافقوه في أنه لا يوصف بعصمة ولا توفيق، فحصل اختلافهم على تسميته قوة أو لا . ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم الأصل عندنا في المسألة أن وجود الفعل ولا قوة لمن له الفعل عليه يُبطل معنى الفعل ويصرفه إلى غيره، وكذلك وجود الفعل ممن هو جاهل به، وهو غير جائز . ثم كان الخطاب لازما بسبب العلم ، وإن لم يكن حقيقته مما لو طلب يُظفر به فكذلك القدرة والعاجز الذي لا يلزمه الكلفة لفوت ما به يطاق، كما لا يلزم المجنون لفوت ما به يعلم. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then the question between them and al-Husayn has no meaning, because al-Husayn says: everything by which the act of obedience occurs is present with the disbeliever except al-ʿiṣma and al-tawfīq. And they agreed with him that he is not described with ʿiṣma nor tawfīq, so their disagreement amounts to naming it power or not. And there is no power except by Allah. Then the principle with us in the matter is that the existence of the act while there is no power for the one who has the act upon him invalidates the meaning of the act and transfers it to something else, and likewise the existence of the act from one who is ignorant of it is not permissible. Then discourse (khitāb) is necessary because of knowledge, even if its reality is not such that if sought it could be attained; so too is power and the incapable (ʿājiz) who is not obliged with burden (kulfa) due to the loss of what enables power, just as the insane person is not obliged due to the loss of what enables knowledge. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a dismissal of the dispute between the Mu'tazilites and al-Husayn (likely al-Najjar) regarding power. Al-Husayn claims that a disbeliever has everything needed for obedience except 'isma and tawfiq, and the Mu'tazilites agree that the disbeliever is not described with these. The only disagreement is whether to call that remaining capacity 'power' or not. Maturidi says this is trivial. He then states his own principle: if an act exists without power for the agent, the meaning of the act is invalidated and attributed to something else. Similarly, an act from an ignorant person is impermissible. He then parallels obligation (khitab) with power: just as obligation is based on knowledge (even if that knowledge is not directly attainable), power is required for obligation. The incapable person (ajiz) is not obligated because he lacks the means of capacity, just as the insane person is not obligated because he lacks the means of knowledge. This reinforces the link between capacity, knowledge, and moral responsibility.

Key Terms
حسين
عصمة
توفيق
قوة
عاجز
مجنون
خطاب
كلفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Maturidi rejects all value in the debate over naming, implying that the entire discussion is worthless.
Correction: Maturidi dismisses only the trivial naming dispute, but he then provides a substantive principle that grounds moral responsibility in the presence of power and knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's principle in this passage, what invalidates the meaning of an act?
A) The act being from a disbeliever
B) The act occurring without power for the agent
C) The act being an act of obedience
D) The act being commanded

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's principle: an act performed without power is not truly the agent's act; similarly, incapability (like ignorance) lifts moral obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever claim credit for an act that you know you did not truly have the power to perform? How does Maturidi's principle challenge your sense of ownership over your deeds?
?
When you fail to obey, do you honestly examine whether you lacked the power or knowledge needed, or do you simply blame yourself? What would it mean to accept that some failures are not truly yours?
?
If the insane are not held accountable because they lack knowledge, how does that affect your view of those with mental illness? Are you quick to judge others without considering their capacity? What would it take for you to extend that same grace to yourself?

Refuting Al-Ka'bi on the Ugliness of the Impossible Command
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi by narrowing the definition of energy and citing the Moses example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of al-Ka'bi's claim that imposing what cannot be borne is self-evidently ugly, focusing on the narrow definition of 'energy' and the counterexample of the companion of Moses.

Core Idea
Al-Ka'bi's 'energy' only means physical health; God's command to Fir'awn proves otherwise.

Al-Ka'bi asserted that commanding what is impossible is inherently ugly. Maturidi responds by showing that this claim relies on an overly narrow definition of 'energy' as mere physical ability. He then provides a concrete example from the Qur'an to demonstrate that God does impose such commands.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on how Maturidi frames al-Ka'bi's claim.
Notice that the claim is presented as a 'first principle' (بديهة).
2
Identify the counterexample
Find where Maturidi cites the companion of Moses (Fir'awn). Understand why this example refutes al-Ka'bi.
Maturidi says God commanded him despite knowing he could not obey.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider what this means for the concept of 'energy' (طاقة). Maturidi limits al-Ka'bi's claim to a narrow, physical definition.
This sets up the broader discussion about God's omnipotence and justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر ما ذكره الكعبي مما يبين وهمه في قضاياه زعم أن تكليف ما لا يطاق قبيح في العقل بالبديهة. وهذا إنما هو في العقل الذي لا يعرف الطاقة غير القوة الظاهرة وهي الصحة، وأما غيرها فليس كما يقول، بل كلف الله صاحب موسى بما يعلم أنه لا يستطيع مثله وكذلك تكليف ما يُجهل مثله في البديهة ، فمثله الأول. ثم يقال له: وكذلك تكليف ما لا يطاق لوقت الفعل قبيح في العقل والذي ادعيته من القبح إنما هو في عقول من يحيل وجود الفعل ولا قوة، وذلك وقت الفعل. فصار قوله عند التحصيل هو القبيح في العقل إن صدق فيما ادعى . ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأما الأصل أن تكليف من منع عنه الطاقة فاسد في العقل. وأما من ضيّع القوة فهو أحق من أن يكلف مثله؛ ولو كان لا يكلف مثله لكان لا يكلف إلا من يطيع، وليس ذلك شرط المحنة. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وعندنا أن القدرة في الصحيح السليم، إذ هي تحدث تباعاً على قدر حرص العبد واختياره وميله إليها، فما لم يحدث لم تحدث بتضييعه؛ إذ اثر بدله واختار الفعل الذي يدفعه . ولا قوة إلا بالله . وعلى مثل هذا التقدير عندنا وعندهم أمر الفهم والعلم. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then we mention what al-Ka'bi mentioned, showing his error in his claims: he claimed that imposing what cannot be borne (taklif ma la yutaq) is ugly in the intellect by first principles. This is only in the intellect that knows no energy other than the apparent power, which is health. As for other than that, it is not as he says. Rather, Allah imposed on the companion of Moses what He knew he could not do similarly, and likewise imposing what is unknown like it in first principles, so it is like the first. Then it is said to him: likewise, imposing what cannot be borne at the time of the action is ugly in the intellect, and what you claimed of ugliness is only in the intellects of those who consider the occurrence of the action impossible and no power, and that is at the time of the action. So his statement upon analysis becomes that the ugly in the intellect is if he is truthful in what he claimed. And there is no power except through Allah. As for the principle that imposing on one who is prevented from energy is invalid in the intellect. As for one who wastes his power, he is more deserving to be imposed upon similarly; and if he were not imposed upon similarly, then none would be imposed upon except those who obey, and that is not the condition of trial. And there is no power except through Allah. And according to us, power in the healthy sound person occurs successively according to the measure of the servant's effort, choice, and inclination towards it. So as long as he does not bring it about, it does not occur due to his wasting it; because he chose its opposite and chose the action that repels it. And there is no power except through Allah. And upon such estimation according to us and them is the matter of understanding and knowledge. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage introduces and refutes al-Ka'bi's claim that imposing what cannot be borne is self-evidently ugly. Maturidi argues that this relies on a narrow understanding of 'energy' as mere physical health. He gives the counterexample of the companion of Moses (possibly Fir'awn), whom God knew could not believe, yet still commanded him. Thus, imposing what cannot be borne is not necessarily ugly. Maturidi then distinguishes between those prevented from power and those who waste it; the latter are still obligated because trial requires possibility of both obedience and disobedience. Finally, Maturidi states the Ash'ari/Maturidi position that power arises from the servant's choice and effort, and if he chooses the opposite, power does not occur.

Key Terms
تكليف ما لا يطاق
قبح
بديهة
طاقة
قوة ظاهرة
صحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any command to do the impossible is inherently ugly and cannot come from God.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the ugliness depends on the definition of 'energy' and that God did command Pharaoh, who was unable to obey, thus such commands are not inherently ugly.

Checkpoint
What example does Maturidi use to refute al-Ka'bi's claim that imposing what cannot be borne is self-evidently ugly?
A) The companion of Moses (Fir'awn) whom God commanded despite knowing he would not obey.
B) The angels who always obey.
C) The prophets who are infallible.
D) The believer who is given power.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi by showing that his claim only holds under a narrow definition of energy and is contradicted by the example of the companion of Moses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that a command of Allah is beyond your capacity, only to later realize that your understanding of your own 'energy' was too narrow? What did that realization demand of you? Will you now examine your assumptions before labeling a divine command as 'impossible'?
?
When you face a situation where Allah commands something that seems impossible by your own measure, do you trust that He knows capacities in you that you ignore? What would it cost you to obey even when it feels impossible?
?
What if your reluctance to obey certain commands is rooted in a self-imposed limitation, not a real one? Could you identify one area today where you have written off obedience as 'too hard' and commit to re-examining it through Maturidi's lens?
